Patient Information:
On the 15th September 2021, a previously healthy, 22-year-old female of South Asian descent was transferred from a local clinic to the emergency department at King Edward VIII Hospital in Durban, KwaZulu-Natal. The patient presented with a two-week history of recurrent fever and a widespread rash over her trunk and limbs. She also reported severe fatigue, prolonged sore throat, and persistent dry cough that had not improved with initial outpatient management.
History:
The patient noted that her symptoms began quite suddenly with high-grade fevers unresponsive to home antipyretics. Two days after the onset of fever, she developed an erythematous rash primarily affecting her chest and back but later spreading to involve all limbs except for spared areas around the eyes. Concurrently, she experienced an intense headache radiating to her neck but did not notice any photophobia or altered mental status.
In addition to this constellation of symptoms, she reported significant fatigue making daily activities challenging. Her sore throat persisted despite throat lozenges and over-the-counter analgesics. Similarly, her dry cough worsened at night causing disrupted sleep patterns.
The digestive system appeared unaffected aside from decreased appetite; however; there was no nausea or vomiting recorded.
Notably absent were joint pain (arthralgia), oral ulcers which could suggest autoimmune disorders like lupus erythematosus (SLE) nor any genitourinary symptoms such as watery eye discharge often seen Mrs ABC, a 58-year-old G5P4014 retired librarian, was referred to our unit for further evaluation and management of an endometrial adenocarcinoma diagnosed at a tertiary hospital two weeks ago. The diagnosis was made following an endometrial biopsy performed due to intermittent painless light vaginal bleeding that had been persisting for approximately six months.
Mrs ABC experienced menarche at the age of 13 and has been postmenopausal for the past eight years. She has not been sexually active for over ten years and reports no history of sexually transmitted infections. Throughout her life, she attended regular gynecological check-ups but neglected cervical cytology screening after menopause; thus, she never underwent a Pap smear in this period.
A comprehensive review of her gynecological history reveals no personal or significant familial history of gynecologic malignancy or other reproductive tract abnormalities. Her obstetric history includes four full-term pregnancies with uncomplicated vaginal deliveries and one spontaneous abortion in early gestation.
In her general medical background, Mrs ABC is hypertensive since seven years ago and manages it effectively with Amlodipine. Other than hypertension and occasional seasonal allergies treated with antihistamines, she denies any other chronic illnesses or previous major surgeries.
Mrs ABC presented initially to her primary care physician reporting mild per vaginal bleeding episodes occurring sporadically without associated pain or discomfort, gastrointestinal symptoms such as bowel changes or abdominal bloating, nor urinary complaints like dysuria or hematuria. This prompted her primary care provider to refer her to secondary care where pelvic examination revealed an enlarged postmenopausal uterus without notable adnexal masses upon Manual Pelvic Examination (MPE).

Case report 2
A 16-year-old male presented with new onset left-sided focal seizures that began two days prior to his hospital visit. He experienced three episodes before arriving at our emergency department, each lasting between 7-12 minutes and was followed by a period of confusion. Upon arrival at the emergency department, he had another seizure characterized by left-sided tonic-clonic movements involving the face, upper, and lower limbs.
One week before presentation, the patient began experiencing a persistent global headache accompanied by photophobia and ocular discomfort. His headaches progressively worsened over time. He reported neither fever nor recent head trauma but did complain of mild nausea without vomiting.
The patient's past medical history was unremarkable except for an episode of viral gastroenteritis six months prior. There were no known predisposing factors for seizures in his personal or family history. His academic performance remained consistent with no recent decline despite intermittent absenteeism due to his headaches.
Upon examination in the emergency department, the patient appeared acutely ill but was alert postictal state with notable drowsiness and mild difficulty following commands initially which resolved after few minutes of observation. General physical examination was normal; vital signs included a temperature of 36.8°C, blood pressure of 110/70 mmHg, heart rate of 88 beats per minute (bpm), respiratory rate at 18 breaths per minute with an oxygen saturation level maintaining around 98% on room air initially fears during epochs observed mainly restrictive periods related context anxiety attacks moving forward civil noting CNII–XII grossly intact contributing signals classic positional deviation overlapping compare symptoms concluded atypical relative present relations documented inclusive broader exploration prognosis differential rapidly determined pending further labs imaging confirm exact cause case scenario initiated empiric management sequence parallel noted hemodynamic steps assessment onwards ymt symptomatic relief ensured dormancy induced manner slight uncomplicated resultant awoke staggering short stints coherence readiness ongoing schedule continuation compliant subsequent follow-ups monitored through detailed analysis results aligned expected crucial stabilization indicator thus

### Clinical Case: 
A 42-year-old female of Hispanic origin presented on 07/09/2023 with complaints of increased skin pigmentation and generalized fatigue noticed over the last 8 months. Additionally, she reported intermittent episodes of dizziness and a persistent dull abdominal discomfort that intensified over the past year.
Upon arrival at the clinic, her vital signs were recorded as follows:
- Core body temperature: 36.5°C
- Pulse: 78 beats per minute
- Respiratory rate: 18 breaths per minute
- Blood pressure: 105/65 mmHg
During her physical examination, she displayed mild pallor but no signs suggestive of jaundice or cyanosis. Skin examination revealed generalized hyperpigmentation significantly notable on sun-exposed areas such as the face, neck, and forearms. The hyperpigmented changes included prominent darkened patches within the axillae and groin regions. She also exhibited marked darkening of gingival mucosa upon oral inspection.
The patient described her fatigue as being progressively debilitating to the point where it interfered with daily activities including her occupation as a school teacher. The episode duration extended throughout most parts of each day, unaffected by rest or sleep quality.
Additional evaluations pointed out involuntary weight loss despite maintaining a balanced diet over recent months; notably losing approximately six kilograms in four months without trying to lose weight consciously.
Past medical history was significant for type II diabetes mellitus diagnosed five years ago which had been managed through oral hypoglycemic agents with overall good glycemic control (HbA1c consistently around seven percent). There was no history suggestive of adrenal insufficiency or similar endocrine disorders in personal nor family history.
Laboratory investigations initiated included serum electrolytes revealing:
- Sodium level at lower normal limits (137 mmol/L)
- Potassium slightly elevated (5.2 mmol/L)
Other relevant laboratory findings showed:
-Fasting blood glucose levels at higher edge consistent with previously known diabetic status.
-Hematologic profile indicating mild anemia (Hemoglobin concentration recorded at 11 g/dL).

A 35-year-old male patient (weight 75kg, height 180cm, BMI 23.15 kg/m^2) presented to the emergency department with high-grade fever (39.0°C), tachycardia (110 bpm), hypotension (100/60 mmHg), tachypnoea (22 breaths/min), left flank pain, and localized swelling and erythema in the left lower lumbar region. The patient had recently been discharged from a private outpatient surgical center subsequent to an incision and drainage of a lumbar abscess carried out ten days earlier.
The patient's recent medical history is notable for an appendectomy performed four weeks prior due to acute appendicitis. Additionally, two weeks before presenting at the emergency department, he had undergone a cystoscopy for examination of hematuria complaints with conservative management recommended post-procedure.
On initial evaluation in the emergency department, the patient appeared ill-looking and was visibly distressed due to pain in his flank area which he rated as severe. He denied experiencing any chills but reported generalized malaise over the past few days and worsening pain symptoms despite having been prescribed oral antibiotics following his recent lumbar procedure.
Physical examination revealed marked tenderness on palpation over the left lower back region with visible redness and mild tissue edema extending approximately 10 cm in diameter around the site of previous surgery. No fluctuance was appreciated on palpation at this juncture suggesting absence of pus collection superficially. Auscultation demonstrated clear lung fields bilaterally without adventitious sounds although rapid shallow breathing was observed correlated with noted tachypnoea.
Laboratory investigations were immediately ordered including complete blood count which returned elevated white cell count at 18 x10^9/L indicative of infection or inflammatory process ongoing within A 26-year-old male patient with the diagnosis of Sturge-Weber Syndrome (SWS) presented to our clinic, experiencing recurrent severe headaches. He had been suffering from these headaches for the past 3 years. The headache episodes were characterized by a throbbing pain that typically lasted between 4 and 8 hours. These debilitating headaches were somewhat alleviated by non-steroidal anti-inflammatory drugs (NSAIDs) but showed no improvement with other pain management strategies.
The patient's headache was unilateral, primarily affecting the left side of his head and was often described as pulsating in nature. Unlike classic migraine symptoms, there were no accompanying nausea or aura-like symptoms noted during these episodes; however, photophobia and phonophobia were consistently reported during the peak of each headache attack. Physical exercise did not exacerbate his condition.
A comprehensive review of his family medical history revealed no significant prevalence of migraines or chronic headaches among immediate relatives. His medical records disclosed a history of epilepsy since infancy; he experienced his first seizure on day ten after birth, manifesting as generalized tonic-clonic activity which prompted further diagnostic evaluation leading to the identification of SWS through clinical examination and neuroimaging studies.
Physical examination upon admission indicated a well-developed male in apparent distress due to ongoing headache paroxysms but with normal vital signs: blood pressure was noted at 120/80 mmHg, heart rate at 72 beats per minute, respiratory rate within normal limits at 18 breaths per minute, and afebrile with a body temperature reading of 98.6°F (37°C). Neurological examination revealed no focal deficits; cranial nerve functions were intact bilaterally except for reduced sensory perception over areas affected by port-wine stains characteristic associated with SWS—the right cheek extending to the inferolateral orbit.
Previous imaging studies including MRI scans had shown leptomeningeal angiomas predominantly involving occipital regions thought responsible for seizure activity observed since childhood.
Laboratory findings including complete blood count (CBC), metabolic panel comprising liver function tests (LFTs), renal function assessments demonstrated unremarkable values—no electrolyte imbalance detected nor derangements attributable able exacerbations presenting disorder hypersensitivity reactions drugs treatment utilized prescribed regimen period reviewed compliance recorded satisfactorily injections annual influenza vaccinations adhered respectively precludes viral co-factors instigating prolonged exacerbations

**Clinical Case Report:**
Mrs. J., a 32-year-old HIV-negative Caucasian (gravida 3, para 1), presented to our antenatal clinic at 12 weeks gestation for routine prenatal care. Her obstetric history includes one previous term pregnancy which resulted in the delivery of a healthy infant and one spontaneous abortion at approximately 10 weeks gestation with an unknown etiology.
At her initial visit, all standard screening tests were carried out and results were within normal limits. Due to her history, she was advised to observe caution but no additional interventions were deemed necessary at that time.
A McDonald cervix cerclage was performed prophylactically at 13 weeks gestation due to concern over cervical insufficiency identified during an early ultrasound scan which indicated a shortened cervix measuring less than 2.5 cm.
Her antenatal period remained uneventful until she reached approximately 28 weeks plus three days gestation when she reported experiencing mild intermittent lower abdominal pain without any vaginal bleeding or other accompanying symptoms during a routine follow-up visit. She was reassured after an ultrasound confirmed normal fetal growth and adequate amniotic fluid levels, with no placental abruption noted.
However, complications arose four days later when Mrs. J presented urgently to our hospital's maternity unit complaining of severe lower abdominal pain coupled with backache, profuse vaginal bleeding described as bright red in color, and persistent vomiting over the preceding three-hour period. These symptoms led her to suspect possible preterm labor or another complication affecting her pregnancy status.
Crucially, Mrs. J noticed significantly reduced fetal movements shortly before these distressing symptoms began manifesting; having felt vigorous activity late into the night just before onset which resulted in sudden cessation early in the morning hours prior presentation at our facility indicating potential acute intrauterine distress albeit initially subclinical concern surrounding viability wasn't raised..

**Clinical Case Study**
A 47-year-old Caucasian male, a schoolteacher and father of three, presented to the surgery outpatient department with chief complaints of a dull ache in his lower abdomen and groin region, coupled with bilateral scrotal swelling and a sensation of heaviness. The patient reported that the left-sided scrotal swelling began approximately five years ago, while the right-sided enlargement started around three years ago.
He described both swellings as gradually increasing in size over time. He did not report any acute pain or discomfort exacerbated by physical activity or relieved at rest. The patient denied any history of trauma to the genital area, systemic symptoms such as fever or weight loss, urinary symptoms like dysuria or hematuria, or changes in bowel habits.
Regarding his medical history, he noted no previous significant illnesses or surgeries. He was not on any current medications and had no known allergies. His family history was non-contributory; there were no documented cases of testicular cancer or other malignancies among close relatives. Additionally, his personal history did not reveal any factors that might predispose him to genitourinary conditions such as prior infections (e.g., mumps orchitis), undescended testes during childhood (cryptorchidism), fertility issues, lifestyle factors like heavy alcohol consumption or smoking.
On presentation to our clinic for evaluation:
- **Vitals**:
  - Blood Pressure: 128/82 mmHg
  - Heart Rate: 72 beats per minute
  - Respiratory Rate: 16 breaths per minute
  - Temperature: Afebrile at 98.4°F
No signs pointed toward cardiovascular compromise: heart sounds S1 and S2 were normal upon auscultation with no murmurs detected. Respiratory examination revealed clear lungs bilaterally without adventitious sounds suggesting lung pathology such as pleural effusion caused by metastatic disease spread from potential testicular neoplasm typical presentations.

We report a case of multiple facial injuries in a 21-day-old neonate who had been healthy until approximately 75 minutes prior to presentation, when the father was alerted by the baby's agitated crying. Upon entering the nursery, he noticed fresh blood on the right side of the baby's face and promptly assessed for visible wounds. On closer inspection after cleaning, he observed significant trauma characterized by partial loss of both upper and lower right eyelids, substantial injury to the nasal bridge resulting in deformity and mucosal exposure, as well as an extensive ulcerative wound extending from just beneath the medial canthus down to midway along her cheek. There were no eye-witness reports detailing how these injuries had occurred.
In recounting events leading up to discovering these severe injuries, it was noted that about 45 minutes before the incident, the infant had been breastfed by her mother and then placed in an infant cot adjacent to their bed at home. The cot sides were properly aligned with mesh protective barriers zipped securely around it—designed specifically for infant safety. It is also relevant that one end of their bedroom window was slightly ajar (though shielded with a wire mesh) during this period to encourage airflow due climate conditions prevalent locally.
Upon immediate observation following discovery—as would be expected given such acute facial trauma—the child displayed incessant crying indicative both pain & hazard thereby raising maternal anxiety significantly; however there arose no delay whatsoever preceding transport action: within mere moments cooperation ensued between parents beginning suitable concourse toward nearby emergency unit desired treatment urgently rendering first-aid measures complementary till opportunity interfacing said professional services guaranteeing provision further trustworthy handling.
Examination at Pediatric Emergency Center revealed thus multifaceted damage initially noted pertaining facial constituents—that duly verifying inherent swelling accompanied definitive haemorrhagic lesions occluding functional normalcy particular features hitherto commensurate presenting profile effectively:

**Mrs. A.N.: A Clinical Case of Advanced Ectopic Pregnancy**
Mrs. A.N., a 29-year-old, G4P1031 and married, was referred to our hospital for further management of an advanced ectopic pregnancy assessed at 32 weeks and 5 days gestation. The patient had been receiving antenatal care (ANC) exclusively at a rural health center managed by midwife practitioners.
The initial history indicated no notable complications during the first two trimesters. Regular ANC visits showed that Mrs. A.N. received standard prophylactic measures against malaria with sulfadoxine-pyrimethamine administered at intervals consistent with local guidelines to minimize malarial risk during pregnancy.
Prophylaxis against tetanus was followed rigorously; she received three doses of the tetanus toxoid vaccine in accordance with WHO recommendations due to her lack of documentation from previous pregnancies completed outside formal healthcare settings.
For anemia prevention, she adhered to regular iron and folic acid supplementation, starting from her first ANC visit at approximately eight weeks into her pregnancy. At around 28 weeks gestation, laboratory analysis revealed a hemoglobin level of 11 grams per deciliter, considered within normal limits for this stage in pregnancy without signs that would suggest intervention beyond routine nutritional support.
Additionally, Mrs. A.N.'s immunization profile included vaccinations against rubella—administered before conception—and toxoplasmosis serology screening came back negative early on in the ANC process indicating no active or chronic infection through early-to-mid gestational weeks.
Standard serological testing for infectious diseases showed she was negative for both HIV-1/2 antibodies and syphilis as determined through TPHA (Treponema pallidum hemagglutination assay).
Despite thorough prophylaxis and diligent care protocols observed during her pre-natal appointments which were undisrupted by acute illness or adverse obstetric events reports emerged only late into We report the case of a patient aged 32 years old with a personal past history of hypertension diagnosed 2 years ago, currently managed with beta-blockers, and a family history of coronary artery disease in the father. The patient was followed for arrhythmogenic right ventricular cardiomyopathy/dysplasia (ARVD) since 4 years ago when the patient experienced an initial episode of palpitations coupled with post-exertional syncope.
The patient presented to the outpatient clinic detailing recurrent episodes of palpitations that were notably pronounced during physical activity and resulted in occasional dizziness. An echocardiogram performed at that point revealed significant right ventricular enlargement alongside regional wall motion abnormalities. Cardiac MRI corroborated these findings, highlighting fibrofatty infiltration particularly affecting the right ventricle and led to suspicion for ARVD.
Subsequent investigations included an extensive cardiac workup: Holter monitoring unveiled frequent premature ventricular complexes (PVCs), averaging about 3,500 PVCs per day predominantly originating from the right ventricle, as well as runs of non-sustained ventricular tachycardia (VT). Additionally, during a stress test on a treadmill Nabors’ clinical criteria confirmed persistent polymorphic VT which resulted in early termination due to symptomatic fatigue and lightheadedness.
Given these observations, further electrophysiological testing was conducted comprising high amplification ECG which showed late potentials indicative of delayed myocardial conduction—a hallmark variegation seen commonly in ARVD. The left ventricular ejection fraction was estimated cautiously at 50%, suggestive marginal compromise; akinly attributable secondary structural cardiac adaptation due to prolonged histrionic arrhythmic events originally from RV origin transforming into hemodynamic performance receivership changes.

A 68-year-old woman was admitted to our department in September 2022, presenting with a history of progressive abdominal distension over the past three months. She reported no accompanying symptoms such as nausea, vomiting, changes in bowel habits, weight loss, fatigue, fever or other systemic complaints. The patient had no significant past medical history and took no regular medication.
Physical examination showed a significant enlargement of the abdomen predominantly on the left side due to an evident mass. There were no palpable lymph nodes in her neck or elsewhere; her cardiovascular and respiratory examinations were unremarkable. Full examination included careful palpation of all quadrants which revealed a firm and non-tender mass extending from the left abdominal quadrant towards the umbilicus. No hepatosplenomegaly was noted.
Hematological tests came back within normal limits including complete blood count (CBC), renal function tests (RFTs), liver function tests (LFTs) and serum electrolytes. Tumor markers including CA-125, AFP, CEA and beta-HCG were also measured to be within normal ranges.
Ultrasonography of the abdomen highlighted a large heterogeneous mass measuring approximately 10cm x 8cm located on the left ovary while leaving other structures intact without any notable abnormalities. Specifically:  
- The liver showed typical echotexture without any focal lesions.
- Gallbladder appeared normal without gallstones.
- Pancreas presented with standard dimensions free from noticeable lesions.
- Both kidneys demonstrated adequate size bilaterally with preserved corticomedullary differentiation.
- Spleen was unremarkable in size exhibiting homogenous echoes.
- Ureters and bladder appeared healthy devoid of calculi or obstructing masses.

A 50-year-old married male presented to the department of otorhinolaryngology with the chief complaint of a gradually enlarging mass located on the left side of his neck for the past 4 months. The patient reported associated discomfort and pain localized over the swelling, particularly noticeable when turning his head or swallowing. Furthermore, he experienced mild difficulty in swallowing solid foods due to the presence of this mass. 
Importantly, there was no record of fever, significant weight loss, loss of appetite, night sweats or any signs suggesting malignancy or systemic infection. Additionally, he denied experiencing headaches, vertigo, tinnitus (ear ringing), ear discharge or any form of trauma preceding this condition. There were no symptoms suggesting respiratory distress such as cough, hemoptysis (coughing blood), shortness of breath or hoarseness in voice.
On past medical history he mentioned seasonal allergies but had not suffered from any notable infections like tuberculosis and presented no history indicative for other chronic diseases like diabetes mellitus or hypertension.
The patient's social history did not suggest behaviors such as tobacco smoking or alcohol use that might contribute to his condition though he occasionally used antihistaminics during allergy season but was generally compliant with medications prescribed by his primary physician.
Clinical examination revealed a firm but mobile solitary nodule roughly 3 cm x 2 cm palpated at level II on the left cervical lymph node chain exhibiting mild tenderness upon deep palpation. Skin overlying nodule appeared unremarkable without erythema warmth consistent with inflammation nor any signs pointing towards abscess formation.

We report the case of a 45-year-old female teacher who presented to the gynecological outpatient clinic of Riverside General Hospital in Kenya with a 3-week history of intermittent post-coital bleeding. She experienced five episodes characterized by bright red blood noticed after sexual intercourse. There was no associated history of vaginal discharge, pruritus, or pelvic pain. 
The patient had been diagnosed with diabetes mellitus type II four months prior to the onset of symptoms but admitted to not being consistent with her oral hypoglycemic medications and follow-up clinic appointments. She reported adherence issues stemming from frequent travel related to her profession and occasional forgetfulness.
There were no significant urinary symptoms such as urgency, frequency, dysuria (painful urination), or hematuria (blood in urine). She also denied any prior trauma or mechanical injuries to her genital area.
Her obstetric history was remarkable; she was gravida 4 para 3+1 (three live births and one miscarriage). Her last menstrual period occurred three weeks before this visit, aligning with a pattern indicating irregular menstrual cycles over recent months. Menarche had commenced at age 12 years, followed by regular menstruations till recent changes that coincided with elevated stress levels at work.
Gynecological examination revealed a middle-aged woman who appeared well-nourished and developed for age without any distress signs noted upon arrival at the consult room.

**Clinical Case:**
Mrs. HSJ, a 42-year-old woman, presents at her outpatient visit on April 20, 2023, with a four-month history of episodic lightheadedness and near-syncope occurring sporadically during various daily activities including sitting at her desk and standing still while waiting in line. These episodes are often accompanied by sudden onset dizziness, mild shortness of breath, momentary blurred vision, and pre-syncopal palpitations lasting approximately five to ten seconds. The episodes typically resolve spontaneously without any intervention.
Mrs. HSJ reports no chest pain or signs suggestive of an acute coronary syndrome during these events. Importantly, she experiences no worsening of symptoms with physical exertion or stress. The absence of dyspnea upon exertion further negates the likelihood of significant underlying cardiovascular compromise directly related to heart failure.
She states that she is generally in good health but acknowledges two significant cardiovascular risk factors: hypertension diagnosed five years ago which she admits has been sporadically controlled due to poor medication adherence; and a family history remarkable for early death from myocardial infarction—her father passed away at age 49 due to his first heart attack likely compounded by undiagnosed dyslipidemia.
From the patient's perspective over the past months prior to this appointment, there have been no new lifestyle modifications; however, she notes an increased workload contributing to poorly managed hypertension given suspected work-related stressors influencing compliance with her prescribed antihypertensive regimen (Amlodipine 5mg once daily).
Physical examination revealed:
- General appearance: alert but mildly anxious middle-aged female in no apparent distress.
- Vital Signs: Blood pressure readings consistently elevated averaging around 155/95 mmHg across multiple visits documenting inadequate control status compared against goal BP targets below <130/80 mmHg; Pulse irregularly regular rate ranging between upper boundary thresholds approximating Tachypneic values (~100 beats per minute).
- Cardiovascular assessment demonstrates S1/S2 regular rhythm without additional murmurs/rubs indicative anginal processes contemporary metadata EKG continuous telemetry.

**Case 2**
A 45-year-old Moroccan man, with a history of essential hypertension and underlying obesity, was diagnosed with sarcoidosis in October 2015 based on clinical radiological findings as well as histopathological confirmation from a lymph node biopsy. He presented to our department in November 2016 for evaluation of exertional dyspnea that had been developing over the course of six weeks and had progressively worsened. This shortness of breath was not associated with fever, chest pain, or syncope. The patient denied any history suggestive of coronary artery disease or thyroid abnormalities. He mentioned that he adhered to a low-sodium diet and took prescribed antihypertensive medications regularly.
The patient reported being a nonsmoker and only an occasional drinker, consuming alcohol less than once per month. On physical examination, the man was alert and cooperative but appeared moderately dyspneic during conversation without signs of acute distress or cyanosis. His vital signs included a blood pressure reading within his usual range under medication control (140/85 mmHg), heart rate at 88 beats per minute (regular rhythm), respiratory rate at 20 breaths per minute, temperature within normal limits at 36.8°C (98°F), and oxygen saturation measured at rest showing mild hypoxemia with SpO2 level of 94% on room air.
Upon further inspection through auscultation, there were bilateral crackles heard predominantly in the mid-lower lung fields without any wheezing or stridor appreciable upon both inspiration and expiration phases; cardiac sounds were unremarkable devoid of murmurs or extra heart sounds detected during routine cardiovascular assessment.
Recent investigations revealed stable renal function tests corresponding creatinine levels around baseline values typically expected for his age group undertaking anti-hypertension therapies adjusted **Case N°2**
A 52-year-old Ethiopian man with a history of systemic lupus erythematosus (SLE) was referred to the internal medicine department for evaluation of severely elevated blood pressure, measuring 215/135 mm Hg, and decreased urine output quantified at 200 cc/day. He also complained of worsening fatigue and shortness of breath over the past week.
The patient's medical history dates back to 2004 when he started experiencing characteristic symptoms consistent with SLE. Over the years, he has been under regular follow-up at the rheumatology clinic where his condition was managed with hydroxychloroquine and low-dose prednisone. The patient initially presented with malar rash, photosensitivity, oral ulcers, and non-erosive arthritis involving both large and small joints. Further laboratory investigations confirmed the diagnosis by identifying positive Anti-dsDNA antibodies along with hypocomplementemia (C3 level: 45 mg/dL) and episodic proteinuria.
In addition to SLE-related complications, his past medical records indicate chronic issues such as hypertension for which he was maintained on amlodipine but had intermittent adherence due to socio-economic barriers in accessing medication. His latest hemoglobin measurement indicated normochromic normocytic anemia (Hb: 10 g/dL).
On physical examination at presentation to our department, the patient appeared chronically ill-looking with a BMI of 18 kg/m² suggesting undernutrition likely secondary to gastrointestinal involvement from his primary disease process. His vital signs showed severe hypertension of 215/135 mm Hg despite being on antihypertensive therapy recently prescribed during an emergency visit last month.
The patient's respiratory rate was elevated at 23 breaths per minute; saturation levels were available showing oxygenation within normal limits at room air though lung auscultation revealed bilateral basal crackles indicating probable fluid overload secondary either due straightforward renal impairment or possibly lupus nephritis flare-up contributing via accelerated hypertension-induced Case 2
A 55-year-old female patient was hospitalized with a complaint of persistent abdominal discomfort and intermittent nausea that had been present for approximately six months. The patient denied any significant events in her medical history and stated that her family history was unremarkable for similar symptoms or major illnesses. She reported no alcohol consumption but admitted to smoking, quantified as 20 pack-years.
On physical examination, the patient exhibited jaundice and notable hepatomegaly measured at 3 cm below the costal margin. Additionally, splenomegaly was also observed, measuring approximately 5 cm below the left costal margin.
Her laboratory findings were as follows: 
- Glucose: 90 mg/dl
- Creatinine: 1.0 mg/dl
- AST (Aspartate aminotransferase): 104 U/L 
- ALT (Alanine aminotransferase): 98 U/L 
- ALP (Alkaline phosphatase): 145 U/L 
- GGT (Gamma-glutamyl transferase): 400 U/L 
- LDH (Lactate dehydrogenase):174 U/L
- Total bilirubin:7.1 mg/dl
- Conjugated bilirubin:6.7 mg/dl
 - Albumin:3.4 g/dL
Further history revealed that she had experienced weight loss of about five kilograms over this six-month period without intentionally changing her diet or activity levels.
Given her presentation of jaundice combined with hepatomegaly and splenomegaly, differential diagnoses included obstructive liver conditions such as choledocholithiasis or malignancies like cholangiocarcinoma, autoimmune hepatitis, or primary sclerosing cholangitis among other potential hepatobiliary pathologies.
To pursue a definitive diagnosis further imaging studies were ordered including an ultrasound which confirmed findings consistent with intrahepatic cholestasis featuring dilated bile ducts within the liver along with evidence suggesting chronic parenchymal damage possibly indicative of cirrhosis in initial readings.

**Clinical Case**
A 17-year-old girl, child of a first-degree consanguineous marriage, presented to our neurology clinic with a history of progressive motor and cognitive decline. At the age of 4, her parents began noticing difficulties in her coordination and fine motor skills. By the time she reached age 7, she had lost the ability to walk independently. The patient has been under treatment with baclofen for spasticity management due to severe extra pyramidal symptoms caused by an undiagnosed neurodegenerative disease.
Her developmental milestones were initially within normal limits until the onset of symptoms at age 4. After various evaluations and notable deterioration in speech and movement abilities, she was withdrawn from school during her primary education due to these increasing difficulties.
The patient’s diagnosis is still under investigation but is anticipated to be Spinocerebellar Ataxia (SCA), considering clinical features such as cerebellar ataxia with both axial and appendicular involvement, dystonia predominantly affecting proximal muscles, ocular abnormalities including oculomotor apraxia observed on examination, along with significant signal changes in the cerebellum as identified through cerebral magnetic resonance imaging (MRI). Genetic studies are ongoing given that familial history includes similar presentations: three older siblings passed away during infancy from unspecified neurodevelopmental disorders exhibiting rapid deterioration—a pattern suggesting autosomal recessive inheritance typically seen in conditions like SCA or Leigh-like syndromes.
Therapeutic interventions thus far have focused on symptomatic support: physiotherapy aiming at maintaining joint mobility despite immobility; speech therapy directed towards improving articulation hindrances caused by marked dysarthria; nutritional support addressing challenges posed by impaired swallowing reflexes consistent across meal intake durations resulting frequently in aspiration The patient was a 22-year-old female primigravida who presented with severe lower abdominal pain and contractions to her community health clinic. She was believed to be at 38 weeks gestation based on an irregular menstrual cycle, and had attended two antenatal appointments (out of the usual four recommended). The patient's medical history revealed no significant issues, however, she did report undergoing surgery for appendicitis in her childhood. Routine screening tests returned negative results for HIV, hepatitis B, and syphilis. During the intake interview, she reluctantly admitted to having ingested a homemade concoction of "natural herbs" purported by local practicers as being capable of inducing labor.
Upon examination at the health clinic, fetal heart tones were noted within normal range but there were persistent intense contractions indicating active labor. Cervical exam demonstrated full dilation with a vertex presentation identified. After approximately three hours in active labor supported by local midwives and nursing staff, she delivered vaginally without instrumental assistance or complications to a live newborn weighing 3.2 kg with Apgar scores of 8 and 9 at one minute and five minutes respectively.
Shortly postpartum, the patient experienced heavy vaginal bleeding suggestive of postpartum hemorrhage (PPH). Initial management included fundal massage which resulted in an expulsion of clots; however,the bleeding continued heavily despite these measures.
Vital signs indicated tachycardia with a persistent heart rate around 110 beats per minute along accompanied by noticeable weakness and pallor on assessment . Blood pressure was measured initially at100/65 mmHg but soon after readings dropped precipitously suggesting hemodynamic instability .

**Clinical Case Report: Orofacial Pain and Temporomandibular Dysfunction in a 51-Year-Old Man**
A 51-year-old man was referred to the unit of orofacial pain and temporomandibular dysfunction at the university medical dental clinic of Lisbon, Portugal. He presented with a progressive limitation in mouth opening and persisting facial pain that had been worsening over the past 18 months.
The patient first reported an insidious onset of pain localized mainly to the right pre-auricular region, which later spread to involve both sides. The masticatory myalgia he experienced was more pronounced in both masseter muscles and temporalis muscles, coupled with significant morning stiffness extending across his jawline. These symptoms were notably exacerbated by activities such as chewing, yawning, and speaking for extended periods.
During an initial consultation, the patient described intermittent clicking sounds emanating from his left temporomandibular joint (TMJ), particularly noticeable during mouth opening or lateral jaw movements. He denied any history of direct trauma to the TMJ or previous infections that might have influenced his symptoms. Additionally, there were no reports of systemic illnesses such as rheumatoid arthritis or other autoimmune conditions that could portend TMJ involvement.
Upon clinical examination, it was observed that maximal mouth opening measured only 22 mm interincisally without significant deviation to either side but with associated discomfort predominantly on muscle palpation bilaterally. Palpation revealed tenderness most accentuated over the masseter muscles on both sides along with moderate tenderness in bilateral temporal areas; however, there was greater discomfort infliction upon deep palpation notably on initial muscular contact after rest—indicative vividly under early-day scenarios echoing morning stiffness claims consistently reiterated by our patient.

A 58-year-old female patient, librarian by profession, reported to the outpatient department of Oral Medicine, Diagnosis and Radiology with a chief complaint of persistent discomfort and occasional pus discharge in relation to the right lower back tooth region for the last 5 months. The patient described the onset as gradual with a history that dates back five months when she first noticed a mild discomfort in her jaw which progressively worsened over time.
The past medical history revealed that the patient was asymptomatic six months ago. She first observed intermittent episodes of dull pain around her right lower molar area that would intensify on mastication but did not completely subside with rest. Over time, this pain evolved into more consistent discomfort accompanied by occasional pus discharge which mainly occurred while applying pressure on the region during brushing or eating.
When taking further dialog history, it was revealed that two-and-a-half months prior to her consultation visit, she began experiencing an unusual tingling sensation followed by numbness on the right side of her face. This numbness initially presented mildly but gradually became more pronounced leading to considerable concern over time.
Moreover, upon inquiry regarding lifestyle and occupational habits, it was found out that due to her profession as a librarian involving extensive periods spent reading and cataloguing books indoors under artificial lighting conditions coupled with irregular eating schedules due to job demands had likely contributed towards neglecting regular oral hygiene practices resulting in potential dental issues culminating in present condition being evaluated today.
At clinical examination:
- Extra-oral: Facial asymmetry was noted owing primarily due localized swelling present extending from mandibular angle upwards along body mandible till zygomatic prominence area thus corroborating patient's complaint regarding facial sensory abnormalities (numb sensation).
- Intra-Oral: Inspection revealed inflamed gingival tissue surrounding distal root aspect specifically concerning second permanent molar (tooth #47). **Clinical Case Report**
**Patient Information:**
A 45-year-old Filipino woman was admitted to the hospital for rehabilitation following a significant accident. The patient experienced a fall from a height two months prior to admission, which resulted in multiple injuries, including spinal cord injury and moderate traumatic brain injury. She displayed no behavioral or psychological problems during her initial six-week hospital stay. After achieving substantial progress toward her rehabilitation goals, the patient was scheduled for discharge.
**Initial Hospitalization and Rehabilitation Course:**
The patient was brought to the emergency department following her fall, presenting with severe lower back pain and restricted movement in both legs. Imaging confirmed a thoracic spinal cord injury and an associated moderate traumatic brain injury characterized by brief loss of consciousness at the scene of the accident.
Upon stabilization in the intensive care unit (ICU) for two weeks, she was transferred to general orthopedics care where she received standard treatment protocols including corticosteroids for edema control around the spinal cord and supportive therapies aimed at controlling pain and preventing secondary complications such as deep vein thrombosis (DVT). 
After four weeks on general ward care focused on wound healing, vital stability maintenance, physical therapy initiation as well as cognitive assessments post-brain trauma event showed reliable recovery without signs of severe neurological deficits.
Upon completion of critical medical management phases she commenced comprehensive inpatient rehabilitation which primarily included regimented physiotherapy sessions targeting locomotion recovery, occupational therapy for adaptive skills development coupled with speech therapy aiming at any potential subtle cognitive processing delays noticed during evaluation rounds post-TBI trauma incident.

**Case Report**
A 58-year-old woman presented to our emergency department with complaints of debilitating fatigue, persistent fever, and shortness of breath that had persisted for three weeks prior to admission. Her medical history was significant for Type 2 diabetes mellitus and controlled hypothyroidism. She had no known drug allergies or other chronic illnesses. During this period, she reported experiencing chest discomfort and arthralgia mostly in the knees and wrists.
Empiric treatment with azithromycin had been initiated at another clinic based on initial suspicions of a respiratory tract infection; however, her symptoms persisted despite completing the course of antibiotics. She also reported an unintentional weight loss of approximately 4 kg over the past month.
Upon admission, her vital signs were as follows: blood pressure was 130/85 mm Hg, pulse rate was elevated at 110 beats/minute, respiratory rate was slightly increased at 22 breaths per minute with mild tachypnea noted upon examination. Oxygen saturation on room air was within normal limits at 98%. Febrile spikes were recorded intermittently with temperatures reaching up to 38.5°C.
Physical examination revealed several concerning findings: a high-pitched diastolic murmur best heard over the aortic valve area during auscultation and fine inspiratory crackles could be detected bilaterally in both lung fields. Peripheral edema involving bilateral lower extremities extending up to mid-shins indicated possible fluid retention or heart failure etiology as well.
A comprehensive laboratory workup showed leukocytosis (white blood cell count: 15x10^9/L), elevated inflammatory markers (C-reactive protein: 12 mg/dL; erythrocyte sedimentation rate ESR -<70 mm/h), elevated random blood glucose levels above baseline despite usual dose adjustment through oral hypoglycemic agents indicating potential stress hyperglycemia due ongoing infectious/inflammatory response mechanisms involved which might need further intervention/control measures if glycemic ranges exceed recommended thresholds beyond short durations advised treatment schedules administered accordingly thereafter since medication reconciliation reviewed guided insulin protocols follow-up monitoring synopsis assessment devised accordingly etc..

Observation 2: Mr. J.M. African American 34 years (in 2023) with a past history of Type II Diabetes Mellitus diagnosed in January 2015, hypertension controlled with medication since February 2016, and an appendectomy performed in July 2018. He has been hospitalized from the 12th to the 19th of October for a chronic progressive muscle weakness affecting both lower limbs which started in early September.
Clinical examination at admission revealed symmetrical paresis of both lower limbs with muscle strength quoted at 1/5 proximally and 2/5 distally. There were absent patellar reflexes bilaterally and Achilles reflexes were also absent, suggestive of severe neuropathy or myopathy. The patient displayed a positive Tabouret's sign upon testing, indicating significant proximal muscle weakness that worsened upon standing.
Mr. J.M reported that his symptoms began as mild fatigue in his legs after climbing stairs which progressively worsened over weeks to the point where ambulation became extremely difficult without assistance by early October. He described no upper limb involvement but noted intermittent episodes of tingling sensations in his feet before significant weakness set in.
Past medical records reveal consistent follow-ups for diabetes management with HbA1c levels averaging around seven percent over the last two years although recent levels spiked to eight point five percent indicating poorer glucose control likely due to reduced physical activity from growing muscle weakness.

A 45-year-old male was admitted to our department with a 3-month history of lumbar radiculopathy—predominantly affecting the left leg—and chronic lower back pain, which had been managed with NSAIDs (non-steroidal anti-inflammatory drugs) only achieving partial relief. One month prior to admission, the patient began experiencing rapidly progressive weakness in both legs along with urinary retention and bowel incontinence. His medical history was notable for successfully treated hypertension but otherwise unremarkable.
On admission, the patient was in mild distress (Karnofsky Performance Status 60%) with significant left-sided paraparesis (3/5), a sensory level below T10, and clear genito-urinary dysfunction. Visual inspection revealed slight muscle atrophy on the left side when compared to the right.
Upon physical examination, vital signs showed: blood pressure of 135/85 mmHg, heart rate of 82 bpm regular, respiratory rate at 16 cycles per minute, and an afebrile temperature of 37°C. Neurological examination revealed reduced strength graded as follows according to standard medical muscle testing scale: iliopsoas muscles graded at 3/5 bilaterally; quadriceps femoris at 4/5 on right and no contraction (0/5) made on left; ankle dorsiflexors were also affected showing strength rated as right -4 /5 and no movement (-1 /5)) noted on left . We present a case of a 50-year-old male who presented at the accident and emergency department of Maun General Hospital, Maun, Botswana. He was referred from a smaller rural clinic. He reported an intermittent gnawing pain in the left iliac fossa that has been progressively worsening over the last year. The patient had no associated vomiting or alteration in bowel habits but did mention mild diarrhea episodes occasionally without blood or mucous. He noted no significant weight loss during this period.
The pain began mildly about twelve months ago and escalated gradually to the point where it interfered with his daily activities and ability to work on his farm. Despite multiple visits to various healthcare facilities where he was treated symptomatically with analgesics and antibiotics, there was no substantial alleviation of symptoms.
On physical examination at Maun General Hospital, he appeared mildly distressed by discomfort but exhibited no signs of acute illness such as fever or jaundice. His vital signs were stable: Blood Pressure 124/78 mmHg, Pulse Rate 82 beats per minute, Respiratory Rate 18 breaths per minute, and temperature 36.8°C.
Abdominal examination revealed tenderness on palpation in the left iliac fossa area yet without rebound tenderness or guarding suggestive of peritonitis. On deeper palpation there was noted a firm mass which seemed tethered but non-pulsatile measuring approximately 9 cm.
Initial investigations including complete blood count revealed mild anemia (Hb 11 g/dL), slightly elevated white cell count (WBC=12 x109/L) indicating some chronic inflammatory process likely ongoing although other differentials could not be ruled out.
Subsequent ultrasound imaging exposed an irregular hypoechoic lesion extending from deeper within pelvis rising towards colon’s splenic flexure probed during sonographic exam causing distinctive localized discomfort again peculiar for its sizable measure (about: ~10cm lengthwise).

Case 2: Mrs. B, a 32-year-old patient in her second pregnancy, was referred at 18 weeks gestation with a diagnosis of Congenital Heart Disease (CHD). She had been diagnosed with moderate Aortic Stenosis (AS) six weeks prior to her referral; however, she had experienced minor symptoms sporadically since adolescence that were previously attributed to less severe conditions. An echocardiogram revealed an aortic valve area (AVA) of 0.9 cm² and an Ejection Fraction (EF) of 55%. The left ventricle was observed to be mildly hypertrophied with no indication of significant pulmonary hypertension.
Mrs. B's medical history included no previous surgeries or interventions for her heart condition but highlighted episodes of palpitations and exertional dyspnea that occurred intermittently over the past years. Additionally, she reported occasional dizziness and fatigue which intensified during periods of physical stress or emotional strain but were generally manageable until the current pregnancy.
Given her complex cardiovascular status and the associated risks posed by pregnancy on a pre-existing cardiac condition, an MDT was convened involving leading specialists including a cardiologist, cardiothoracic surgeon, obstetricians specialized in high-risk pregnancies, anesthesiologists knowledgeable in cardiac cases during pregnancy, along with additional support from maternal-fetal medicine experts.
Assessment by the cardiology team reaffirmed the need for close monitoring throughout Mrs. B’s gestation period due to potential risks including arrhythmias or exacerbation of AS-related symptoms under physiological stressors typical in late-stage pregnancy. It was decided that regular transthoracic echocardiograms would be essential every four weeks to track any significant hemodynamic changes as well as monthly visits for clinical evaluation assessing symptom progression such as shortness of breath or chest pain.
The obstetrics division planned perinatal care inclusive of detailed monthly growth scans ensuring fetal health compromised neither by maternal cardiovascular challenges nor medication adjustments should they become necessary following evolving status reports on Mrs. B's AS severity levels.
Considering labor and delivery presented substantial hemodynamic load demanding precise management strategies tailored individually towards both mother’s wellbeing alongside optimal childbirth outcomes — several scenarios were anticipated based on simulations provided by analysis from cardiothoracic concert meetings within MDT setups:
1) Vaginal Delivery under Epidural Anesthesia: Recommended if gravid stabilization remained satisfactory thereby minimizing invasive exposure shifting concerns tied around excessive blood loss post-Caesarean drawbacks whilst providing controlled analgesia effectively tempering circuitous demands.
 
2) Elective Cesarean Section: Primary contingency premised on deteriorating findings suggesting disproportionate risk efficacies warranted expedient measures circumventing undue straining exclusively The patient, a 14-month-old girl named Emma, was seen in our out-patient clinic due to several congenital anomalies that had been noticed immediately after birth. Her mother additionally brought up concerns regarding Emma's apparent difficulty in opening her mouth widely, both when she cries and during feeding attempts. Unlike in some families where blood relation might shed light on such occurrences, Emma's parents are not related by blood.
Emma is the third child of her mother; her first two children were born without any congenital anomalies. No history exists of relatives with similar conditions on either side of the family. Her pregnancy history was largely uneventful: there were no complications during gestation, and prenatal assessments showed normal fetal development. Labor and delivery also progressed smoothly without any notable issues.
During the neonatal period at the hospital where she was born, her initial assessments indicated multiple anomalies which have necessitated ongoing follow-up evaluations ever since.
Upon examination today, Emma presented as alert but irritable; she had not exhibited fever ('afebrile'), jaundice ('anicteric'), central or peripheral cyanosis ('acyanosed'), which might signify oxygen deprivation or other circulatory problems; nor did she show signs of respiratory distress that would indicate breathing difficulties—she seemed comfortable in terms of breathing parameters. Notably though, despite being normotonic (having normal muscle tone), it’s evident from both direct observations and reports provided by the parent about challenges linked notably to facial musculature involvement potentially affecting jaw mechanics.
Specific physical symptoms observed included micrognathia (an undersized jaw), a common feature connected to existing trouble with achieving an open-mouthed state effectively enough for practical **Clinical Case**
A 5-year-old girl presented to the emergency department with symptoms of increased intracranial pressure, including headache, vomiting, and altered level of consciousness. Upon thorough examination and imaging studies, it was determined that she had hydrocephalus due to a malfunctioning ventriculoperitoneal (VP) shunt. Initial attempts to revise the VP shunt were unsuccessful due to technical challenges and considerations related to her medical history; therefore, an alternative cerebrospinal fluid diversion method was necessary. Consequently, she received treatment with a ventriculoatrial (VA) shunt percutaneously inserted through the internal jugular vein.
The patient had been diagnosed at five months old with a supratentorial primitive neuroectodermal tumor (sPNET), grade IV according to the World Health Organization classification. At that time, she presented initially with developmental delays and seizures. A brain MRI revealed a 5 cm × 4 cm mass in the right frontal lobe causing significant obstructive hydrocephalus.
Following her initial diagnosis at five months old, she underwent surgical resection of the tumor followed by aggressive chemotherapy according to standard pediatric oncology protocols for high-risk PNETs. Due to complications related to her ongoing treatment and disease progression, a VP shunt was placed when she developed symptomatic hydrocephalus one month postoperatively.
Her condition stabilized over two years of varying levels of medical intervention until her recent presentation in the emergency department.
Upon arrival at our facility during this latest episode, her physical exam included notable signs: lethargy but reactive pupils bilaterally; papilledema on fundoscopic examination; and an unsteady gait observed while walking into triage somewhat disoriented compared normoactive state proximate times earlier periods questioned relatives close preceding symptoms onset incident occasion before noted discrepancies current emergent situation regarding behavioral inconsistently frequency occurrences particular episodes retrospectively appreciative observation slight subtle changes movement coordination overall balanced regularly given serious complex concerning notedly acutely exacerbated problematic evaluation timeline window antecedent problem occurrence temporarily variable extent shift symptomatic concerns referral warranted focus here

We present here a case of Hemophagocytic Lymphohistiocytosis (HLH) associated with Chediak-Higashi syndrome in a 4-year-old female born of consanguineous parentage. Her birth histories, family social history, and developmental milestones were unremarkable.
She was born at full term following an uneventful pregnancy with no antenatal or perinatal complications. The patient was exclusively breastfed for the first 6 months, at which time food diversification was initiated. Growth parameters such as weight and length were consistent with normal ranges over the course of her early development, and psychomotor development appeared age-appropriate. Described by her caregivers as having a healthy appetite, she adhered to a normal diet and exhibited appropriate thriving behaviors.
The patient presented to our clinic for evaluation due to persistent high-grade fevers over the past two weeks. Associated symptoms included fatigue, lethargy, pallor, abdominal distension, and intermittent vomiting. There was also noted hepatosplenomegaly upon physical examination.
Her medical history revealed several episodes of recurrent infections including otitis media and lower respiratory tract infections which necessitated antibiotic therapy multiple times over the last year. Detailed familial history did not reveal any similar illnesses; however, consanguinity suggested a potential underlying genetic predisposition.
Laboratory investigations demonstrated pancytopenia with hemoglobin levels markedly decreased at 7 g/dL (normal range: 10-14 g/dL), platelets reduced to 30 x10^9/L (normal range: 150-450 x10^9/L), and neutrophil counts significantly lowered at less than 1 x10^9/L (normal range: more than1.5 x10^9/L). Liver function tests revealed elevated transaminases: ALT at 150 U/L (reference <55 U/L) and AST at 180 U/L (reference <45 U/L). Ferritin levels soared beyond standard values exceeding >500 μg/mL suggesting significant systemic inflammation or secondary tissue damage.
Further diagnostic work-up included bone marrow aspiration which showed macrophages actively phagocytosing erythrocytes along with other blood elements indicating hemophagocytic lymphohistiocytosis (HLH). Genetic screening illuminated mutations pertinent to Chediak-Higashi Syndrome confirming the diagnosis genetically corroborated by lysosomal trafficking regulator protein abnormalities seen under electron microscopy (“giant granules”).
To address this critical condition promptly standard HLH treatment protocol involving dexamethasone intravenous infusion coupled initially traded cyclosporin - agressive immunosuppressive drugs started encouraging prompt steroid tapered slow improvement clinical status marked afebrile catchup growth noted recovery phase inputted nutritionally optimized health unit 
Our concerted multidisciplinary engagement recommend stem cell transplantation deemed definitive curative prospective case specialist contrapuntal conducted matched unrelated donor commended preparatory individualized regimens meticulously pre-treatment chemotherapy schedule aimed successful engraftment maintaining systemic stability perceived priority steering clear potentiality profound exacerbate sequelae
In conclusion crucial bring collective proficiency forefront unravel intricate rare pediatric enigmatic aligned utmost diligence addressing complexities humbly stride surmounting procedural hurdles uplifting livelihoods intrinsic caretaking humanely spearhead enriched future-worthy pursuits Following initiation of the standardized treatment protocol for hemophagocytic lymphohistiocytosis (HLH), involving dexamethasone intravenous infusion, there was an expedited incorporation of cyclosporine to manage the aggressive immunosuppression needed. Initial responses to treatment indicated a positive trajectory with a noticeable reduction in systemic inflammatory markers, accentuated by declining ferritin levels. The patient's hematologic parameters showcased gradual normalization with hemoglobin incrementing towards baseline; thrombocyte counts and neutrophil figures improved concomitantly.
Throughout this vigilant therapeutic phase, interdisciplinary collaboration underscored our efforts to systematically align patient outcomes with established HLH benchmarks. Continuous monitoring revealed transient hepatic enzyme fluctuations, necessitating tailored dosage adjustments of immune-modulatory agents.
Parallelly, dietary inputs were critically appraised to support anabolic demands underpinning recovery and catch-up growth. Dietitians proposed nutrient-dense formulations fostering hematopoietic recovery and ameliorating cachectic tendencies hitherto observed amidst protracted illness periods.
Given the child’s resilience towards initial pharmacologic management yet recognizing the exigency for definitive cure preemptively staving off potential relapses inherent in HLH alongside Chediak-Higashi Syndrome (CHS)'s genetic undercurrents, stem cell transplantation (SCT) was endorsed forthrightly as curative intent therapy. A comprehensive HLA typing matched an unrelated donor deemed perfect reducing allo-immune conflict risk profiling complementing myeloablative conditioning contour dictated pre-transplant chemotherapy regimen conceptualized propitious engraftment milieu pursuits.
Post-transplantation convalescence encompassed multifaceted surveillance deftly orchestrated by our bone marrow transplantation unit ensuring systematic immunologic riser balancing fortified engraftment assurance minimizing graft-versus-host disease implications through strategic administration calcineurin inhibitors along adjunctive methotrexate taper regulated meticulous follow-up intervals stringent infection prophylaxis protocols instituted safeguard vulnerabilities innate post-myeloablative milieu enduring resilient immune reconstitution enhancement periodic evaluations continued affording comprehensive healthcare continuum addressing nuanced clinical exigencies Rehabilitation modalities inclusive encompassing structured physiotherapeutic engagement combined psychosocial supportive mechanisms addressing holistic child-centric rehabilitative approach crucial ensuring quality life optimality socio-emotional reintegration realms uplifting sustenance proficiently entwined fostering recuperative milieu endearing potential long-haul remission sustained longevity specter imbibed life nurturing vibrancy propitious equally aspirational capstone enriched outcomes satisfaction shared progressively exceeding conventional longitudinal prognostic outlines envisaging transformational precedent earnestly esteemed practiced health stewardship confronting rare pediatric disorders tackling exactically bringing hope soaring zenith advocacy translational excellence collaboratively synergized endeavors befitting prime edicts enriching humane service pinnacle elevating universal caregiving paradigms transcending beyond boundaries chronicled path-breaking strides surmounting medical vistas redefining future carescapes transcendent commitment touching lives indelibly nurtures ever-responsive humanity accedes noble discourse
We report a case of a female near-term neonate with gestational age of 37 weeks. She was the first child born to a 25-year-old mother with no significant medical history, except for controlled hypothyroidism managed with levothyroxine. The pregnancy was the result of a second degree consanguineous marriage. The infant was delivered via spontaneous vaginal delivery after an uneventful labor. Clear amniotic fluid was noted upon rupture of membranes. Her birth weight was 2850g (50th Percentile). Upon delivery, the baby exhibited normal extra-uterine adaptation and Apgar scores were 8 and 9 at one and five minutes respectively.
At birth, her physical examination revealed no congenital anomalies or dysmorphic features; however, she experienced episodes of mild respiratory distress characterized by intermittent grunting and nasal flaring despite regular oxygen saturation levels. This transitory respiratory distress resolved within the first six hours post-birth without any requirement for supplemental oxygen.
Given that routine antenatal screening had returned negative results for infections but acknowledging maternal hypothyroidism as a potential risk factor, a detailed neonatal assessment was conducted to rule out any complications secondary to materno-fetal influences.
During her initial hours in the nursery, she maintained normothermia but had slightly elevated resting heart rates between 150-160 beats per minute which necessitated close monitoring. Capillary refill time and peripheral pulses were normal.
Initial laboratory workup including complete blood count (CBC), C-reactive protein (CRP), blood culture, thyroid-stimulating hormone (TSH), free T4 levels did not reveal abnormalities except for mildly elevated CRP suggesting possible subclinical infection or inflammatory reaction potentially linked to minor delivery trauma or suboptimal intrauterine conditions given slight polyhydramnios noted on prenatal ultrasounds during late gestation.
Considering clinical stability yet marginal laboratory deviations supportive antibiotics encompassing coverage for Group B Streptococcus and Gram-negative bacilli were commenced in accordance with neonatal sepsis protocol while awaiting definitive culture results.

**Case Presentation:**
A 27-year-old previously healthy woman presented to the emergency department with a 4-day history of high-grade fever reaching up to 41°C and bilateral lower abdominal pain. The patient's past medical history was unremarkable. She worked as a software engineer and had not been on any form of medication prior to her current illness. The patient lived alone, had no pets, and reported no recent travels or contact with anyone exhibiting similar symptoms.
On initial evaluation in the emergency department, her vital signs showed a heart rate of 95 beats per minute, blood pressure of 122/78 mmHg, respiratory rate of 18 breaths per minute, oxygen saturation of 99% on room air, but she was febrile with a body temperature measured at 39°C. Physical examination revealed tenderness in both lower quadrants upon deep palpation without any guarding or rebound tenderness suggestive of peritoneal irritation. No organomegaly or palpable masses were noted during abdominal palpation.
Systemic examination did not reveal any abnormalities; cardiovascular and respiratory assessment were normal with clear lung fields auscultated bilaterally. Neurological evaluation was unremarkable as well, without any focal deficits noted.
Given her clinical presentation involving sustained fever coupled with abdominal pain localized bilaterally in the lower quadrants as opposed to the right upper quadrant observed in previous similar cases such as hepatobiliary disorders; it warranted an extensive differential diagnosis which included but was not limited to acute appendicitis extant (especially involving pelvic appendix), gastrointestinal infections leading towards diverticulitis considering age-associated epidemiology alongside genitourinary pathologies like pyelonephritis considering potential complications aforementioned within secondary care prompting urgent imaging contingency follow-ups accordingly if deemed clinically justified progressively escalating discerned diagnostic approach here onwards...

Title: A 35-year-old Pakistani Female Engineer with Recurrent Long Bone Fractures and Suspected Primary Hyperparathyroidism
Introduction:
A 35-year-old Pakistani female engineer presented for an orthopedic consultation, with a history indicative of possible primary hyperparathyroidism. Over the span of four years, she experienced serial fractures of several long bones under minimal traumatic circumstances.
History:
The patient’s initial encounter with fracture occurred when she fractured her right tibia after losing balance and falling down a single step. The fall was trivial, involving no significant external force or velocity. She received conservative treatment involving immobilization which was expected to promote healing by six weeks.
Unfortunately, within another five months before the tibial fracture achieved symptomatic resolution, she sustained another injury—this time fracturing her left radius while attempting to open a jammed window at her home. The mechanism involved no excessive force but resulted in severe pain followed by swelling in the left forearm prompting immediate medical attention.
The sequence continued four months later; the patient fell again during routine gardening work causing another serious injury—fracture of left femur due to tripping over low garden fencing. Noteworthy is that non-operative management involving casting from prior tibial and radial fractures had not culminated in uniform healing as expected despite attentive compliance to advised therapy regimes.
Six months subsequent to femur-wise affliction—and while still conserving constrained mobility—the patient encountered another debilitating incident leading to right humeral shaft fracture following On the 14th of March, 2023, a 42-year-old female patient reported to the Department of Prosthodontics at Green Valley Dental Clinic, Los Angeles. Her chief complaint was that she had been experiencing discomfort in her upper left molar that recently underwent root canal treatment three weeks prior. She described a dull pain exacerbated by biting and chewing and noted slight mobility in the affected tooth.
Upon initial examination, it was observed that there was swelling in the gingival region surrounding the suspect tooth without any evident pus discharge. Radiographic images were taken to provide a detailed assessment of the treated area. The periapical radiograph revealed satisfactory root canal filling with no signs of periapical pathology nor any external or internal resorption.
Next, clinical diagnostic tests were performed to rule out any fractures or underlying issues not visible on radiographs. Trans-illumination and staining using gentian violet dye were employed to identify possible vertical or horizontal fractures that could have escaped initial detection during previous treatment sessions. Following thorough investigation with these methods as well as careful probing around circumferential margins of periodontal ligament space with an explorer tool, no fractures were detected on either lingual or buccal aspects (Figure 1).

An 21-year-old Hispanic female, with no significant past medical history, presented to the emergency department at Ben Taub Hospital in Houston, Texas with complaints of persistent chest discomfort. The patient described the symptoms as an intermittent chest discomfort that she had been experiencing for a duration of approximately six weeks. This discomfort typically occurred in the early hours of the morning and lasted for about 5 minutes each time.
The chest discomfort was non-radiating and not sharp or severe but was notable enough to cause her some concern. She denied any palpitations, dizziness, sweating, or shortness of breath accompanying these episodes. There were also no instances of pre-syncope or syncope experienced by the patient; neither was there any pain aggravation related to physical activity nor relief upon resting.
Additional history revealed that her appetite remained normal with no current issues related to food intake such as heartburn or acid reflux-like symptoms. She had also not experienced any respiratory-related triggers like coughing fits or exacerbation by deep breaths during these episodes.

Case 2: A 65-year-old female patient had been admitted to our department with a one-month history of progressively worsening headache and slight difficulty in coordinating limb movements. She also had a three-month history of minor head trauma from accidentally hitting her head on the corner of a cabinet. Cranial CT revealed a left-sided chronic subdural hematoma (CSDH). The patient’s past medical history was significant for well-controlled type 2 diabetes mellitus and chronic kidney disease stage III, managed with dietary modifications and metformin (Table 1). Routine systemic examinations were performed preoperatively, including chest radiography, electrocardiogram, and complete blood work.
Upon initial examination in the emergency department, the patient reported experiencing mild to moderate headaches that started to disturb her sleep approximately two weeks prior to admission. She described the headaches as dull and spread across her entire frontal region but noted aggravation on the left side. In addition, she noticed occasional episodes of dizziness and clumsiness in both hands while attempting everyday tasks such as writing or buttoning clothes.
Neurologically, she showed signs suggestive of mild weakness in both upper limbs along with reduced coordination; nonetheless, no focal motor deficits were identified during physical examination. Vital signs at presentation included blood pressure within normal limits (128/76 mmHg), pulse rate at 78 beats per minute, respiratory rate at 16 breaths per minute while maintaining an oxygen saturation level around 97% on room air.
Laboratory findings performed upon admission displayed no remarkable deviations except for slightly elevated serum creatinine concentration indicative of baseline chronic kidney insufficiency without alarming progression noted concurrently. Hemoglobin A1c value reflecting proper glycemic control was recorded at acceptable levels compatible with measured fasting blood glucose readings between ranges anticipated under prescribed maintenance medication regime conditions.

indefinitely specified .
We report a 34-year-old woman, with no significant past medical history, presenting with a two-month history of dry cough and worsening shortness of breath. Her symptoms were insidious in onset but progressively intensified over the past few weeks. Additionally, she reported fatigue, night sweats, and an unintentional weight loss of approximately 5 kilograms over the last two months.
A non-smoker, she worked as a preschool teacher within an urban setting and had no known exposure to respiratory pathogens or environmental pollutants. She denied any recent travel history or contact with individuals diagnosed with tuberculosis. Her family history was non-contributory regarding respiratory illnesses.
The patient’s initial physical examination revealed tachypnea with a respiratory rate of 28 breaths per minute alongside mild cyanosis around her lips. There was diminished breath sound bilaterally upon auscultation without wheezes or rales noted explicitly at that time. The remainder of her systemic examination findings were unremarkable.
Chest X-ray revealed bilateral interstitial opacities predominantly in the lower zones without consolidation or pleural effusion which suggested the need for further diagnostic evaluation given her rapidly worsening clinical presentation.
Arterial blood gas (ABG) analysis performed on ambient air demonstrated marked hypoxemia; pO2 measured at 60 mmHg while pCO2 remained within normal limits at 40 mmHg indicating primarily parenchymal involvement rather than ventilatory failure reflected by HCO3- slightly elevated at 27mmol/L possibly compensating metabolic alkalosis secondary to chronic under-perfusion status further validated through concurrent SpO2 reading displaying saturation level dipping down into mid-80s percentiles aligning aptly between existing symptomatic complaints correlatively necessitating aggressive escalation towards supportive management immediately tabled before proceeding additional anatomy-specific radiographic modalities namely High Resolution Computed Tomography (HRCT).
