**Clinical Case:**
A 52-year-old woman of Nigerian-origin was admitted with a five-week history of progressive bilateral ankle edema associated with aches and occasional muscle spasms while walking. She reported an unintentional weight loss of approximately 8 kilograms (kg) over the past four months. The patient described a three-week history of intermittent night sweats accompanied by low-grade fevers and generalized fatigue. In addition, she experienced acute onset dyspnea one day before admission, which initially manifested during physical exertion but gradually progressed to being present even at rest by the time she reached the hospital. 
She did not have accompanying symptoms such as cough or chest pain. Despite receiving multiple courses of oral antibiotics in outpatient settings, there was minimal clinical improvement leading to her eventual decision to seek further evaluation.
The patient's past medical history included hypertension for the last ten years managed with amlodipine, but no known renal disease or diabetes mellitus. She reported no significant family medical history and denied any recent travel outside her home country.
On initial examination at presentation, the patient appeared clinically stable without signs of distress; however, notable findings included peripheral cyanosis and tachypnea with a respiratory rate of 28 breaths per minute and oxygen saturation persistently fluctuating between 88-92% on room air. Heart rate was measured at 110 beats per minute, blood pressure recorded as 135/85 mmHg while her basal temperature stood mildly elevated at around 37.8°C (100°F).

History 1: A Complicated Fever and Reluctance Towards Intervention
November 2, 2023: The patient, a local merchant named Mr. AB (52 years old), resides in the coastal town of Taiara. Mr. AB has one wife (EB) and two children. On November 2, EB developed symptoms consistent with infectious viral disease—fever reaching up to 39°C (102°F), significant lower abdominal pain, continuous diarrhea, and episodes of projectile vomiting.
Initial management consisted of oral rehydration solutions given at home; however, her condition deteriorated rapidly within hours due to hypotension and escalating worsening of symptoms. Despite family objections driven by cultural misconceptions about hospitals being places where "people go to die," EB was transported from Taiara Regional Health Post to the Infectious Disease Treatment Center (IDTC) in Tanora during the evening hours under close observation and using full personal protective equipment per protocol.
Upon arrival at IDTC on November 2nd late night:
- Rectal temperature recorded at admission: 38.6°C.
- Vital signs reported as BP: 88/57 mmHg; HR: 121 bpm; RR: high variability between normal intervals.
- Physical examination noted considerable dehydration characterized by dry mucous membranes, poor skin turgor along with deeply sunken eyes.
Immediate laboratory investigations included:
1.) Blood cultures sent for bacterial pathogens.
2.) RT-PCR specific for Filoviruses initiated given endemic background now seen around Taiara during past few weeks.
3.) **Clinical Case Report**
Patient Overview: An extended family composed of members from a rural village in the Al-Madinah Region, Saudi Arabia. This family has been under observation by our department for concerns related to congenital adrenal hyperplasia (CAH) due to 11β-hydroxylase deficiency (11β-OHD). 
**Patient 1:** A 29-year-old female (46, XX karyotype), originating from a consanguineous marriage, was diagnosed with 11β-OHD at the age of four. The condition first drew medical attention due to signs indicative of peripheral precocious puberty, particularly clitoral enlargement and early pubic hair development. In addition to these observations, advanced bone age was noted upon radiographic examination.
Laboratory findings at diagnosis were highly suggestive of 11β-OHD. The investigation revealed elevated levels of serum 11-deoxycortisol and adrenocorticotropic hormone (ACTH), coupled with suppressed plasma renin activity values ​(see Table 1).
Joining an introductory description regarding her background and primary diagnostic features is pertinent for understanding the full extent of the patient’s history and ongoing management.
---
### Presenting Complaint
## History
Patient Background:
- **Age/Gender:** The patient is currently a 29-year-old female.
- **Geographical Origin:** From a rural area in the Al-Madinah Region within Saudi Arabia.
- **Family History & Consanguinity:** Both parents are first cousins; hence there exists a significantly increased risk for autosomal recessive genetic conditions within this lineage.
Early Developmental Concerns:
At around four years old, her mother noticed early-onset puberty symptoms manifesting as clitoral hypertrophy along with rapid growth spurt relative to her peers. Subsequently, clinical evaluation was pursued when she displayed premature development marked by accelerated linear growth and pubarche.
Diagnostic Workup Initial Findings:
At four years old on presentation:
- Blood pressure: elevated reading consistent with hypertension
- Serum biochemistry showed markedly increased concentrations of:
   - Serum 11-deoxycortisol: >500 ng/dL 
   - ACTH: >200 pg/mL 
   - Plasma renin activity: Low-suppressed activity

Case 2: A 22-year-old married college student, reported to the outpatient Department of Oral Medicine and Maxillofacial Radiology with a chief complaint of persistent pain and swelling in the lower right jaw area. She was fairly built and nourished, exhibiting average stature. She revealed no significant systemic illness or detrimental habits such as smoking or alcohol consumption.
On general physical examination, the patient appeared well oriented and conscious. Notably, she exhibited very sparse eyebrows with a generalized hair thinning pattern throughout her scalp. There was no history of heat intolerance or issues related to excessive sweating; however, she did report occasional dry skin episodes that necessitated frequent moisturizing.
The patient’s fingernails were brittle with noticeable transverse grooves (Beau’s lines) across several nails on both hands. Additionally, there was evident koilonychia (spoon-shaped nails) similar to iron deficiency anemia presentations despite her reporting no known anemic conditions previously diagnosed. On close inspection, her toenails also exhibited mild deformities though less pronounced than those observed in her fingernails.
During oral examination, extraoral findings included slight facial asymmetry owing to swelling on the right lower region near the angle of mandible extending anteriorly towards midline but not crossing it. Palpation indicated tenderness with a localized firm mass palpable subcutaneously underneath relatively normal-looking overlying skin without any ulceration or significant color change.
Intraoral examination revealed moderate gingival inflammation primarily around posterior teeth along buccal aspects bilaterally more apparent adjacent involved quadrant suggesting localized A 52-year-old man with a history of Hepatitis C and chronic liver disease of 12 years duration presented with increasing fatigue and progressive shortness of breath on exertion over the past year. He denied any cough, chest pain, orthopnoea, or paroxysmal nocturnal dyspnoea. However, he reported experiencing occasional palpitations but no episodes of syncope or dizziness.
His past medical history was notable for well-controlled type 2 diabetes mellitus diagnosed 10 years ago and hypertension managed effectively for the last five years. It was also noted that he had been followed up for portal vein thrombosis diagnosed three years ago during a routine imaging study. Since then, the patient has been on anticoagulation therapy with rivaroxaban.
Additionally, the patient was diagnosed with Hepatitis C twelve years ago and has been receiving antiviral treatment including sofosbuvir and ribavirin for this condition over the last decade. Despite consistent compliance to his medication regimen, his chronic liver disease has progressed to cirrhosis as indicated by laboratory results showing elevated liver transaminases and imaging studies revealing liver surface irregularities.
On examination at presentation, he appeared fatigued but in no acute distress. His vital signs were within normal limits except for mild tachycardia with a heart rate of 102 beats per minute. The respiratory rate was steady at 18 breaths per minute while oxygen saturation on room air measured at 96%. Physical examination revealed mild pallor; however, there were no signs suggestive like jaundice or cyanosis observed.
Cardiorespiratory examination revealed an irregularly irregular heartbeat confirming atrial fibrillation without additional abnormal heart sounds such as murmurs or rubs detected upon auscultation; lung fields were clear bilaterally without crackles or wheezes appreciated either auscultatorily nor audiophonically In addition to his cardiorespiratory assessment revealing extensive workup results correlating hepatic encephalopathy detecting ammonia levels distinctly higher than normorelated indexes patients physical stature determining moderate-sized palpable spleen inferential diagnosis splenomegaly nonpalpable stigmata noting peripheral edema gauge indicating minimal engagement distended findings reversal reveal confident health notwithstanding thorough evaluation capitalizing informative determined hepatology consultants strategize intervention cumulatively therapeutic discourse typeof both factor resource reliance stepwise planned prioritize alleviate symptomatic burden complex pathophysiological attributes retaining regular scheduling monitored outpatient continued following ensuing pertinent continuity thereafter reinforce efficacious successful presumably preeminently compatible habilitative predominant cohesiveness retained collectively oqoportunity demonstrate improved outcome indeks systematic functionalist oriented principle context comprehensive cluster equanimity professional integration cooperative disciplined towards manageable preferential welfare additionally

**Clinical Case Report**
A 32-year-old female reported to the department of medicine and oral surgery with the chief complaint of excessive gingival display during smiling. She expressed concern about her smile aesthetics, feeling self-conscious during social interactions. There were no significant medical or family histories that could adversely affect treatment outcomes, and she presented with no existing medical conditions that could contraindicate a surgical intervention.
On clinical examination, her face was bilaterally symmetrical. Upon inspection, it was noted that she had slightly incompetent lips at rest. Intraorally, a substantial amount of attached gingiva was observed throughout both maxillary and mandibular arches. The patient was diagnosed with a prominent case of "gummy smile."
When the patient smiled naturally, her teeth were visible from the maxillary right second molar to the maxillary left first premolar with an impressive 6-7 mm of gingival display across the upper anterior segment. The maxillary anterior teeth exhibited normal anatomical proportions relative to their crowns and roots.
Upon further evaluation using cephalometric analysis and photographic records in various positions (resting lip position versus full smile), it became apparent that an altered passive eruption might be contributing to her condition as well as potentially hyperactive upper lip muscles.
Given these findings after a thorough discussion about available treatment options including their risks and benefits; orthognathic consultation for potential osseous surgery versus non-surgical periodontal interventions—she opted for less invasive management initially through comprehensive gingivectomy followed by potential Botox injections for further refinement if necessary afterward Second case: An 80-year-old male patient with a history of chronic obstructive pulmonary disease (COPD), type II diabetes mellitus, chronic kidney disease, and hyperlipidemia who had undergone coronary bypass surgery was referred to the oral medicine and oral surgery department at City Health Hospital (Austin, Texas) because of persistent pain and swelling localized in the left mandibular region. The extraoral clinical examination revealed the presence of one palpable left submandibular lymph node greater than 1.5 cm with a firm consistency.
The patient's medical history is significant for multiple comorbid conditions. His COPD has been managed with long-term bronchodilator therapy and intermittent corticosteroids during exacerbations. He has been insulin-dependent for diabetes management over the last ten years and is on dialysis thrice weekly due to advanced-stage chronic kidney disease. His medication regimen includes metformin, insulin lispro, atorvastatin, albuterol inhaler, prednisone during exacerbations, aspirin for coronary artery disease prevention post-bypass surgery, amlodipine for hypertension control, along with calcium carbonate as a phosphate binder secondary to renal insufficiency.
The onset of his current problem began approximately three weeks ago when he noticed mild discomfort in his left jaw which gradually escalated into severe pain associated with noticeable swelling impeding regular functions such as eating and speaking.
Consequently referred by his primary physician considering his complex medical background necessitating specialized evaluation by our department here at City Health Hospital's Oral Medicine unit. Upon presenting today; The patient reported significant difficulty masticating due toward increasing intensity involving deep-seated throbbing sensations affecting lower facial regions suggesting underlying potential pathology needing detailed assesment both medically dentally.
A comprehensive intraoral examination performed revealed relatively poor dental hygiene partly attributed towards limitations imposed on routine activities managing numerous health parameters concurrently per individualized care recommendation priorities balancing multi-faceted criticality across diseases spectrum officially registered under different specialties coordinating through electronic health records (EHR). A 25-year-old single female presented to the emergency department with a compound fracture of the right femur, deep lacerations on her left arm, and a severe contusion on her forehead following a fall from a height of approximately 15 feet while rock climbing. The patient experienced extreme pain in her right lower extremity and left upper extremity, along with mild dizziness but no loss of consciousness. She had no significant medical history except for occasional migraines. On arrival, she was noted to be alert and oriented but displayed signs of distress due to pain. 
Her initial vital signs were stable: blood pressure was recorded at 130/80 mm Hg, pulse rate was 115 beats per minute (likely elevated due to pain), respiratory rate was 20 breaths per minute, and oxygen saturation was within normal limits at 98% on room air.
During the physical examination:
1. **General Appearance**:
   - The patient appeared anxious and in obvious discomfort.
   - There were no signs of cyanosis or jaundice.
2. **Head and Neck**:
   - A substantial contusion covered her forehead without any deep lacerations or skull deformities.
   - Pupils were equal, round, reactive to light and accommodation (PERRLA).
   - No neck stiffness or visible external injury around the neck region.
3. **Chest**:
   - Her chest wall movements were symmetric.
   - Breath sounds were clear bilaterally without wheezes or crackles.
4. **Cardiovascular System**:
       Heart sounds S1/S2 heard; no murmurs detected upon auscultation.
       Peripheral pulses palpable equally bilaterally.
5. **Abdomen**:
    Abdomen soft; non-tender across all four quadrants.
    No organomegaly detected upon palpation.

- The patient's skin exhibited generalized pallor. There were no signs of cyanosis, jaundice, or petechiae.
- Aside from the contusion on her forehead and the swelling on her right thigh and wrist, there was no additional noticeable bruising or discoloration on other parts of the body.
9. **Genitourinary System**:
- No visible external injuries in the pelvic region.
- Urination is reported to be normal with no dysuria or hematuria.
10. **Psychosocial Assessment**:
- The patient demonstrated mild anxiety, attributed to the traumatic event leading to injury.
- Family support appeared adequate; a spouse was present at bedside expressing concerns but also providing reassurance.
### Initial Diagnosis and Plan
Based on current assessment findings:
1. **Diagnosis**:
   - Traumatic Contusion of Forehead: Primary facial trauma without underlying skull injury as indicated by lack of deformity and preserved neurological function (PERRLA).
   - Right Thigh Hematoma with Suspicion of Hemarthrosis Above Knee Joint: Evident swelling suggesting soft tissue injury needing radiographic examination for potential bone involvement.
   - Suspected Musculoskeletal Injury Involving Wrist/Hand: Indicating active bleeding around biceps tendon area, stabilized temporarily but requires orthopedic evaluation.
2. **Initial Management Steps**:
   - Continued pain management including morphine administration for acute relief.
   - IV saline infusion initiated to prevent hypotension and maintain adequate perfusion particularly given noted pallor suggestive potential volume depletion post-injury shock state stabilization **Case 2: Baby KJ**
Baby KJ, a female infant from the Efik tribe, was delivered at term in a regional hospital by spontaneous vaginal delivery to a 27-year-old para 1 mother. The baby cried well at birth and weighed 3.2kg. At the age of approximately three hours postpartum, she was referred on account of absence of skin and skull bone over a specific region noticed during the routine neonatal examination.
The pregnancy was supervised with regular antenatal care visits starting from the first trimester. The mother denied any use of medications other than prescribed antenatal vitamins and supplements, specifically denying any use of orthodox or herbal remedies during her pregnancy. A detailed ultrasound scan performed during her second trimester was reported as normal with no anomalies detected.
Baby KJ is the second child of her parents who are in a monogamous non-consanguineous family setting, having no known biological relations between them except through marriage.
On physical examination upon admission at our facility, Baby KJ presented active crying with good muscle tone and movement consistent with gestational age appropriate for term birth. Vital signs revealed:
- Axillary temperature: 36.7°C
- Heart rate: 140 beats per minute
- Respiratory rate: 50 breaths per minute
- Oxygen saturation in room air: 98%
Inspection showed an area approximately two inches in diameter on the left parieto-occipital region exhibiting complete absence of both skin and underlying skull bone (cranium bifidum). However, there were no other visible anomalies noted including facial dysmorphisms or limb deformities.
A thorough systemic review yielded that cardiovascular examination revealed dual heart sounds without murmurs; pulmonary auscultation presented clear breath sounds bilaterally; abdominal examination demonstrated soft consistency without organomegaly or palpable masses; neurological assessment depicted symmetrical movements prompting intact reflexes including sucking and Moro reflexes albeit potential discomfort associated due to exposed cerebrum tissue.
Immediate imaging studies initiated included cranial ultrasound which confirmed localized absence involving superficial exposure affecting minimal brain parenchyma devoid any gross structural deformation but susceptible environment-related complications such as infection risk amplification already managed sterilized dressings post identification promptly under sterile conditions performed neuroprotection intervention reducing incipient sensitivity exacerbations occur seamlessly progressive stages involve multidimensional interventions hasten recovery stabilization proficiently directed specialized consultation neurosurgery deliberating optimal pathway closure preserving structure functionality concurrently preventing further detrimental sequela establishing coherence multi-disciplinary integrative framework collectively responsibly adhered ethical regulatory compliant adjuvant supportive frameworks encompassing volunteering external caregivers communicating transparently shared holistic-parental understanding ensuring sustainable rehabilitative continuum comprehensively securing long-term developmental milestones requisite monitoring assessments therein requisite prophylactic measures inclusively applied stringently following evidence-basis validated research evolving prospects conducive preventive advancements neonatal pediatric healthcare ultimately enhancing survivorship quality standard optimizing equitable equity aligned community-centric strategies thereby committed essence safeguarding precious future generation enfold nurturing compassionate values stewardship treasured innocent lives our humane solemn endeavor prevail transcending dimensions universally righteous humane transcendence epitomizing reflective evidential pioneering clinical excellence dedication persisting toward inexorable revocations inherently boundless aspirations unified healthier radiant exemplar generational perpetuity illustrious humanity ethos! With meticulous precision, immediate interdisciplinary discussions were initiated involving neonatology, pediatric neurology, and infectious disease specialists to devise a cohesive management protocol tailored specifically for the neonate. The sterile dressings applied post-identification were determined efficacious in maintaining a stable barrier to environmental pathogens; however, stringent surveillance for potential nosocomial infections was instated due to inherently heightened vulnerability.
Clinically, bi-hourly assessments documented vital signs consistency: heart rate sustained at 125 beats per minute, respiratory rate steadying at 50 breaths per minute with persistent normoxia (Oxygen saturation: 98% room air). Continuous neurologic monitoring ensured prompt identification of emerging symptoms indicative of compromised cerebral integrity.
Laboratory investigations affirmed fundamental biochemical stability—complete blood counts within normative ranges albeit marginally elevated leukocytes; sterile cultures via cerebrospinal fluid aspiration corroborated unremarkable findings ruling out initial microbial invasion which assuaged preliminary apprehensions concerning septicemia onset.
During the third day postpartum amalgamative consultations underscored initiating preemptive broad-spectrum antibiotics adjunctively fortifying standard prophylactic immunizations discerning prudent risk-avertive measures collaboratively endorsed prioritizing anticipatory critical exigencies safeguard. Concurrent nutritional support emphasized enteral feeding supplementation enriched tocopherols facilitating oxidative defenses bolstering integra-bioenergetics metabolic demands restructuring bronzed fortified prospec development paradigms warrant fostering integral correspondence evolved resilience expansion framework encompassing multidimensional interlaced corporeal fortitudes substantiate pervasive progressive enrich facets usher remiss infusion equilibrial foundational continuum derivations longitudinal capacity transformative aptitudes vest increasingly substantial advancing pioneering epoch reverential neonatal scholarly impetus!

A 57-year-old woman presented with persistent, gradually worsening headaches over the course of three months. These were described as continuous, holocranial headaches that significantly affected her overall performance status. Approximately four weeks later, she returned to the clinic presenting with early morning vomiting and reporting a concerning issue of double vision. Additionally, she noted episodes of dizziness and a general sense of balance disturbances. The patient confirmed having had periodic fevers over the past three months.
Her past medical history was largely unremarkable; there were no known chronic conditions or previous hospitalizations. She was not on any regular medication aside from occasional use of over-the-counter pain relievers for minor ailments. The patient's family history revealed no significant genetic predispositions to neurological or infectious diseases.
Upon her initial presentation to our outpatient department in December 2019 for evaluation of these symptoms, we conducted a physical examination and collected basic vitals: blood pressure (130/85 mmHg), heart rate (78 bpm), respiratory rate (16 breaths/min), temperature (38°C). General inspection showed a well-nourished but visibly fatigued middle-aged woman in mild distress due to her headache.
Neurological examination revealed moderate papilledema suggestive of increased intracranial pressure; however, there were no other distinct focal neurological deficits at that time.
Given the severity and persistence of her symptoms as well as febrile illness with unexplained origin, an extensive workup was initiated:
- **Complete Blood Count (CBC)**: Revealed mild leukocytosis.
- **Basic Metabolic Panel**: Within normal limits.
- **C-Reactive Protein (CRP) & Erythrocyte Sedimentation Rate (ESR)**: Both elevated indicating ongoing inflammation.
- **Blood cultures**: Taken but initially negative.
- **Chest X-ray & Abdominal Ultrasonography**: Unremarkable findings pointing away from common systemic sources.
An MRI brain scan without contrast showed diffuse meningeal enhancement consistent with meningitis-like pathology leading us to consider differential diagnoses including neoplastic processes such as meningeal carcinomatosis versus infectious etiologies like chronic meningitis which encompassed bacterial infections including tuberculosis or fungal causes among others.
Lumbar puncture performed shortly after admission provided cerebrospinal fluid analysis revealing:
* An opening pressure at high end reference 
* CSF pleocytosis predominantly lymphocytes
* Elevated protein content
* Markedly decreased glucose concentration relative to serum levels
These results strongly suggested infective etiology needing immediate intervention pending confirmatory culture results anticipated from CSF samples alongside multiplex PCR panel designed targeting A 45-year-old Kenyan female named Nyambura was referred to Kenyatta National Hospital from her district healthcare center for a thorough evaluation of progressive swelling and discomfort in her left upper arm. The patient has a documented history of type 2 diabetes mellitus, which she has managed with oral hypoglycemic agents for the past decade. Despite some fluctuations in blood sugar control, Nyambura had generally maintained stable health until three weeks before this presentation.
It began insidiously with mild discomfort in her left upper arm, which she initially dismissed as strain due to physical activity. However, the pain escalated over the following days into severe throbbing and burning sensations that gradually spread towards her left wrist. Notably, within two weeks of symptom onset, Nyambura developed noticeable swelling accompanied by skin discoloration.
Nyambura described the pain as relentless and radiating downward into her fingers, significantly impairing daily activities such as cooking and dressing herself. Movement further exacerbated the pain while rest offered only minimal relief. Over-the-counter analgesics provided temporary respite but were overall ineffective at controlling what had become an intensely debilitating condition.
At presentation to Kenyatta National Hospital's emergency department (ED), Nyambura appeared uncomfortable though not acutely distressed. On examination by attending healthcare providers:
• **Vital Signs:**
   - Temperature: 37.3°C
   - Pulse: 92 beats per minute
   - Blood Pressure: 128/82 mmHg
   - Respirations: 18 breaths per minute
   - SpO2: 98% on room air
• **Physical Examination:** 
    - Inspecting both arms revealed significant asymmetry; there was marked erythema extending from just below the shoulder joint down towards mid-forearm on the affected side.
    - Palpation induced pronounced tenderness across swollen areas – especially over muscles around biceps brachii region.
     - No lacerations or discernible points indicating trauma history existed upon closer skin inspection beside general warmth suggestive of possible inflammation/infection component contributing symptomatically overall.
     - Neurological testing yielded slight decreases detected grip strength compared right respectively dimming touch sense across distal regions/exposed digits.
Laboratory investigations conducted included:
• **Complete Blood Count (CBC):**
    - Elevated white cell count indicative systemic infection ongoing effort body's defenses against presumed bacterial pathogen invasion circulatory pathways via localized septic focus found upon **Clinical Case:**
**Patient:** A 65-year-old female who presented with a 10-year history of asymmetrical abdominal enlargement with the left half more noticeably enlarged. 
**Chief Complaint:** Persistent, insidious left-sided abdominal enlargement and difficulty performing daily activities due to perceived imbalance.
**History of Present Illness:** The patient, Mrs. Lydia Thompson, noticed an initial generalized weight gain starting around the age of 55. However, over time this evolved into a marked truncal weight excess predominantly on her left side. This abnormal distribution became progressively prominent and troubling over the ensuing years. By age 60, it was clear that her abdomen was significantly more enlarged on the left side compared to the right.
Despite her identification of this asymmetrical swelling, Mrs. Thompson did not report any acute symptoms indicative of intra-abdominal pathology such as localized pain, nausea, vomiting or changes in bowel habits; thus she initially ignored it attributing it to aging and lifestyle factors.
Due to this uneven weight distribution she struggled greatly with lying flat—both prone and supine postures caused substantial discomfort due to increased pressure on one side which disturbed her sleep routine immensely.
Her considerable symptomatic burden began to manifest functionally around five years ago when driving started becoming arduous as turning sideways or maintaining steady balance while seated resulted in exaggerated tilting towards the heavier side leading ultimately for fear about vehicular safety grounds resulting in cessation driving practices altogether two years back

A 72-year-old male with a history of controlled hypertension and type 2 diabetes mellitus was examined in the Orthopaedic Department of Saint Lazarus Hospital (Florence, Italy). The patient presented with pain and swelling in the proximal phalanx of the 4th finger on his left hand, which is his non-dominant hand. He reported difficulty in flexing this finger, which had progressively worsened over the past three months.
Five years earlier, he underwent a similar episode involving an enchondroma (EC) at the distal phalanx of his right hand's middle finger. At that time, he was treated through curettage followed by back-filling using calcium phosphate cement (CPC). Post-operative radiographic evaluations showed satisfactory progress initially two years after surgery. Notably, during this period following surgery for EC on his right hand, the patient adhered to rehabilitation protocols meticulously and experienced no significant complications.
The patient denied any recent direct trauma to his left-hand fingers and had consistently refrained from smoking or consuming alcohol throughout his life. His overall lifestyle included regular moderate exercise adapted to age-related limitations but sufficient to manage metabolic conditions actively. Moreover, no risk factors such as auto-immune diseases or other chronic joint diseases were identified in past medical records or family history.
On physical examination at this visit for evaluating the new complaint involving another phalanx: 
- The proximal interphalangeal joint presented noticeable swelling.
- There were tenderness upon palpation along both volar and dorsal aspects.
- Limited range-of-motion especially during active flexion was evident alongside a mild palpable mass within soft tissues around affected area.
- Neurovascular assessment remained intact without evidence suggesting any compromise; capillary refill confirmed less than two seconds’ delay indicating unimpaired circulation within digital vessels supplying involved anatomical region.
Initial investigative workup included standardized plain radiography primarily focusing upon various views capturing affected anatomy comprehensively:
1) Anteroposterior view disclosed lucent lesion measuring approximately eight millimeters suggestive indicative recurring pathology similar previously observed enchondromal changes although localized differently,
2) Lateral view depicted cortical thinning adjacent regions reinforcing suspicions underlying degenerative lesion largely contributing functional impairment symptomatic presentation today’s consultation initiated rectifying therapeutic intervention,
Given these findings along preliminary diagnostic impressions concerning transition potentially towards malignant transformation if untreated extending definitive management suggested comprehensive evaluation thoroughly before proceeding surgical considerations,

Clinical Case:
A 30-year-old woman, with a medical history significant only for mild asthma managed with as-needed albuterol inhalation, gravida three para two, presented to the gynecological emergency unit of our university hospital. She was referred from an outpatient clinic due to new-onset hypertension and neurosensory symptoms including persistent headache and occasional episodes of blurred vision. Her pregnancy was estimated at 28 weeks and 3 days of gestation.
Upon initial assessment, the patient was conscious and oriented (Glasgow Coma Scale (GCS) at 15/15). Vital signs were notable for elevated blood pressure readings consistently in the range of 160/105 mmHg. She described her headache as dull yet constant, predominantly localized in the frontal region and unresponsive to over-the-counter analgesics.
Her physical examination revealed no apparent distress but noted bilateral lower extremity edema extending up to mid-calf level. Cardiovascular examination detected no murmurs or rhythm abnormalities. The respiratory system examination was unremarkable; lung fields were clear upon auscultation, consistent with her well-controlled asthma status.
Osteo-tendinous reflexes were brisk but symmetrical, with a noted absence of clonus. Plantar reflexes demonstrated downward flexion bilaterally without any pathological features such as Babinski sign indicating upper motor neuron involvement or neurological impairments suggestive of central nervous system pathology. There were no motor or sensory deficits identified during the clinical evaluation.
Further laboratory workup included complete blood count (CBC), liver function tests (LFTs), renal function tests including serum creatinine levels, uric acid levels; all returned within normal limits except for a slight increase in liver transaminases which could correlate with pregnancy-related physiological changes rather than pathological conditions like HELLP syndrome.
Urine analysis indicated significant proteinuria (+3 on dipstick test) supporting suspicion towards pre-eclampsia despite normal urine output volumes being recorded during our observation period **Clinical Case Study**
A 65-year-old male patient with a history of non-insulin-dependent diabetes mellitus, hypertension, hyperlipidemia, and chronic obstructive pulmonary disease (COPD) attended the oral medicine and oral surgery department at City Haven Hospital (Gothenburg, Sweden) due to persistent pain and discomfort localized in the left maxillary region. The patient's medical history revealed recent medication adjustments intended to improve blood glucose control; specifically, he had transitioned from metformin monotherapy to a combination regimen including sulfonylureas. Additionally, he was taking statins for hyperlipidemia management alongside regular inhaled corticosteroids for COPD maintenance.

Our clinical case is a 12-year-old boy named John, born from a third delivery with prolonged labor lasting 6 hours and maternal preeclampsia that was managed with antihypertensive medication. Delivery was uncomplicated; he had a birth weight of 3,200 grams and measured 52 cm in length. During the neonatal period, multiple abnormalities were observed including macrocephalism, hepatomegaly (enlarged liver), decreased visual acuity diagnosed as retinal degeneration, diffuse osteopenic changes on X-ray, and anemia suggestive of hematologic dyscrasia. Based on these findings and elevated serum calcium levels despite dietary adjustments to limit calcium intake as per consults with the dietitian team for months without improvement; the diagnosis of familial hypocalciuric hypercalcemia (FHH) was made.

Case N°2: 10-year-old patient admitted to the emergency room for sudden onset of severe headache, vomiting, and confusion lasting for 3 hours prior to admission. The hetero anamnesis collected from his family reveals that the patient has a known history of migraines since the age of 7 years, managed with infrequent need for medication as episodes were rare and typically mild. The parents report that this current episode was markedly different due to its intensity and accompanying neurological symptoms.
The parents described that three hours before bringing him in, the child complained of an excruciating headache followed by repeated bouts of vomiting. Shortly after these initial symptoms, he became increasingly unsteady on his feet and disoriented in time and place. This neurological decline culminated in two episodes where he lost consciousness momentarily but regained it within a minute each time.
Upon arrival at the emergency room, initial assessment revealed a lethargic but responsive child with Glasgow Coma Scale (GCS) score of 13 (E3 V4 M6). His vitals were noted as follows: blood pressure at 110/70 mmHg; pulse rate at 98 bpm; respiratory rate at 22 breaths/min; temperature at 36.9°C (98.4°F); oxygen saturation was stable at 99% on room air.
Heteroanamnesis further elucidated that this acute episode was preceded by a five-day history of what seemed like viral gastroenteritis characterized by moderate fever, generalized body pain, diarrhea, malaise treated symptomatically with oral rehydration salts and occasional doses of acetaminophen without significant relief.
The mother cited concern about recent behavioral changes observed over the past week - primarily excessive somnolence during daytime activities which they initially attributed to fatigue from presumed viral infection but now appears might have been early neurological signs warranting prompt evaluation had they been recognized earlier.
A thorough physical examination showed no obvious focal neurological deficits – cranial nerves were intact; motor system examination revealed normal tone & power across all four limbs coupled with A 52-year-old construction worker came to the dermatovenereology outpatient clinic with a chief complaint of multiple raised, rough patches and nodules on his right forearm. The lesions had been present for approximately 15 years before admission. They first appeared as small, pinkish bumps that were soft to touch and slightly itchy but painless. Over the years, the lesions gradually increased in size and number, becoming more noticeable and harder in texture.
Approximately five years ago, these spots began forming purplish nodules which eventually merged into larger plaques interspersed with smaller satellite lesions. The patient reported episodes where these areas were prone to bleeding upon minor trauma such as brushing against rough surfaces at work or during shaving. He also noticed occasional pus discharge mingled with blood from some of the more prominent nodules.
The patient specifically mentioned worsening of symptoms during humid weather but noted no seasonal variation overall. He has tried various over-the-counter creams which provided only temporary relief from itching but did not significantly impact lesion size or severity.
The patient's medical history was remarkable for hypertension managed with lifestyle modifications alone; he took no medications regularly except for sporadic use of antihistamines for seasonal allergies. There was no family history of skin disorders nor any personal or family antecedents suggestive of autoimmune diseases.
He denied having systemic symptoms like fever, night sweats, weight loss, fatigue or chills throughout this period. We present a 9-month-old male who was admitted for tetralogy of Fallot (TOF) repair. The patient had been experiencing recurrent episodes of dyspnea and cyanosis, which were exacerbated by physical exertion such as crying. His medical history includes multiple hospital admissions for similar complaints and a recent upper respiratory tract infection that led to hospitalization where he received antibiotics, showing significant improvement.
The child exhibited marked failure to thrive, weighing only 4.5 kg with a height of 64 cm. Heart rate at the time of presentation was recorded at 150 beats per minute (bpm), respiratory rate was 42 cycles per minute, and blood pressure measured at 75/45 mmHg. Upon examination using auscultation methods, heart sounds S1 and S2 were noted with an ejection systolic murmur heard best over the left upper sternal border along with the splitting of S2.
During chest auscultation, bilateral coarse crepitations were observed predominantly in the lower lung fields reflecting possible infection or pulmonary congestion secondary to heart defect complications. The patient appeared alert but irritable; head circumference (OFC) was within normal limits at 41 cm; pupils equal and reactive bilaterally; no signs of neurological deficits with normal tone globally; muscle strength assessed as power grade four across all limbs indicating mild weakness possibly related to overall poor health condition.

A 7-year-old boy living in a remote village was brought for consultation at the National Center for Tropical Skin Diseases, presenting with persistent ulcers around his lower abdomen and thighs that had been evolving over the past 9 months. The initial signs manifested approximately 5 years earlier as a small, painless but slightly itchy nodule on his left thigh. Over time, this nodule grew insidiously, spreading towards the groin area and buttocks. Eventually, an indurated plaque formed that later ulcerated profusely.
The patient's guardians reported that despite their best efforts using various forms of herbal medicine and several courses of unspecified antibiotics prescribed by local health posts, there had been no significant improvement in the lesions. At times, they noticed transient reduction in size but ultimately saw increased ulceration periods after interrupted treatments or self-medication attempts failed.
The boy's vaccination status was up to date according to national schedules and he was not exposed to riverine environments frequently; rather he played mostly within his yard or at communal play areas within the village. Socially active yet reserved due to chronic discomfort from his condition, he continued attending school until symptoms recently worsened.

- General Appearance: A well-nourished but visibly distressed child.
- Vital Signs: Stable (normotensive), afebrile with mild tachycardia indicative of potential systemic inflammation.

**Clinical Case Report**
**Patient Information:**
A 55-year-old female presented to the neurology out-patient department (OPD) in August 2021, generating significant interest due to her distinctive presentation and the following cascade of diagnostic events and management strategies up to the follow-up period.
**Chief Complaints:**
The patient reported symptoms of persistent dysphagia, occasional nasal regurgitation when consuming liquids, and pronounced dysarthria for a duration of one week. Additionally, she mentioned feeling progressively weaker over several months but attributed it to aging and stress from work.
**Past Medical History:**
The patient's medical history was relatively unremarkable with no notable illnesses. However, she had a diagnosis of type II diabetes mellitus for which she was on oral hypoglycemics (Metformin). Despite having prescriptions for regular blood sugar monitoring and glycemic control, her compliance was sporadic at best as evidenced by her last HbA1c recording being above target levels (8.5%) six months ago. She had no known allergies or family history of neurological disorders.
**Social & Family History:**
The patient lived with her husband and two adult children in an urban environment. She worked as an accountant, involving long hours seated at a desk - conditions often leading to neglecting health check-ups. Her lifestyle included minimal physical activity but no smoking or alcohol consumption.
Upon clinical examination:
- **General Appearance:** The patient appeared moderately obese with a Body Mass Index (BMI) of 31 kg/m^2.
- **Vital Signs:** Blood pressure measured at 140/90 mmHg; heart rate was regular at 80 beats per minute; respiratory rate stood normal at 18 breaths per minute.
 - **Neurological Examination:** 
   - **Higher Mental Functions:** Fully alert and oriented regarding person, place and time; comprehension intact.
   - **Cranial Nerves Examination:** Notably revealed reduced palatal elevation on phonation suggestive of cranial nerve IX/X involvement.
     Pooled secretions were observed indicating potential aspiration risk.
   - Initial GCS score maintained maximum values (15/15).
- Motor functions displayed slight bilateral weakness in upper extremities graded as muscle power IV/ V under manual muscle testing criteria without any focal deficits in sensory evaluation.
Initial differential diagnoses considered included cerebrovascular event presenting typically with bulbar symptoms or neuromuscular junction disorder such as myasthenia gravis given fluctuating weakness pattern described by the patient consistently worsening by end-of-day activities necessitated confirmatory diagnostics:
Laboratory Tests:
- Routine blood tests including Complete Blood Count (CBC), electrolytes panel showed mild normocytic anemia otherwise unremarkable markers except elevated fasting glucose level >180mg/dL confirming poorly managed diabetic status,
Electrophysiological Studies:
Repetitive nerve stimulation study indicated decremental response reinforcing initial suspicion towards neuro-muscular pathology essentially pointing towards bulbar variant Myasthenia Gravis further validated through positive serum Anti-acetylcholine receptor antibodies assay results subsequently establishing firm diagnosis,
Image Studies:
Brain MRI scan ruled out acute CNS pathological changes inclusive definitive differentials like infarcts“
Treatment Course Initiated:-

We hereby report the uncommon case of a 16-year-old girl patient with no significant medical history who presented with secondary amenorrhea accompanied by intermittent and cyclical pelvic pain persisting for the past five months. The patient reported her menstruation began at age 13 but ceased abruptly one year ago, followed by recurring pelvic discomfort that intensified during what should have been her menstrual cycles.
On initial examination, the patient exhibited a normal body habitus and was of average height and weight for her age. She displayed well-developed secondary sexual characteristics such as pubic hair at Tanner stage V and fully developed breasts corresponding to Tanner stage V. Despite these normal developments, palpation revealed a firm and tender pelvic mass measuring approximately 4 cm in width and 2.5 cm in height.
A thorough vulvo-perineal inspection demonstrated normal external genitalia with symmetrically defined labia majora, intact labia minora, and an anatomically typical clitoris. No abnormalities were observed upon external examination; there were no signs of swelling or discoloration suggestive of inflammation or infection.
Given the findings on physical examination combined with the patient's history of secondary amenorrhea and pain localized to the pelvis occurring cyclically, an ultrasound scan was performed to determine any underlying gynecological pathology contributing to these symptoms. Ultrasound imaging revealed a dilated vaginal cavity filled with echogenic material indicative of hematocolpos – accumulation of menstrual blood in the vagina due to obstruction.
Further diagnostic evaluation involved MRI imaging which confirmed our primary suspicion: transverse vaginal septum obstructing menstrual flow leading to retrograde menstruation or retained menses A 32-year-old man, who, two weeks prior to admission, had started experiencing generalized fatigue and unintentional weight loss of approximately 5 kilograms. This was accompanied by intermittent fever peaking at around 38.5°C and night sweats, which resulted in his admission to our hospital.
On clinical examination upon admission, the patient appeared visibly unwell with signs of severe malaise (WHO stage 2). He reported feeling weak and unable to perform his daily activities without becoming breathless. His body temperature was recorded at 38.2°C during the initial assessment. Unlike previous similar cases, he did not present with lymph node hypertrophy nor any noticeable hepatosplenomegaly upon abdominal palpation.
Cardiopulmonary examination revealed clear lung fields bilaterally without any adventitious breath sounds; heart rates were regular with no murmurs detected on auscultation. The abdominal exam was benign—without tenderness or organomegaly—and there were no ascites observed.
On a neurological level, there were no gross deficits; cranial nerves II through XII were intact on examination; muscle strength was rated as normal (5/5) throughout all extremities despite subjective reports of profound exhaustion.
Biological assessments highlighted significant pancytopenia: a marked reduction in all cell lines based on complete blood count results:
- Hemoglobin level was measured at 7.8 g/dl indicating moderate-severe anemia,
- Mean globular volume (MGV) showed microcytosis with an average volume of 77 fl.
- Platelet count revealed pronounced thrombocytopenia at merely 8000 G/l,
- Total leukocyte count indicated leukopenia standing at just under adequate levels—1340 G/l.
- A closer look into differential counts showed neutropenia (absolute neutrophil count or PNN being borderline low at 690 G/l),
- Concomitantly lymphocyte counts underscored lymphocytopenia distinctly reported as being down to only about half-normal values—520 G/l respectively.
   
Further diagnostic tests included a bone marrow aspiration and biopsy due to persistent pancytopenia indicative findings along peripheral horizons ruling out malignancy such as leukemia or other We report the case of a female patient, 65 years old, diabetic for 15 years on insulin therapy and hypertensive for 8 years on lisinopril; non-smoker with a history of moderate alcohol consumption. She presented with sudden onset of confusion and expressive aphasia that began approximately 24 hours prior to admission. Initial assessment found Glasgow Coma Scale (GCS) score of 13/15 (eye opening at 4, verbal response at 3, motor response at 6). Pupils were symmetrical and reactive to light without any abnormal asymmetric findings. No gross motor or sensory deficits were detected; however, she exhibited hyperactive deep tendon reflexes bilaterally. 
The patient was noted to be tachypneic with a respiratory rate of 26 cycles per minute and an oxygen saturation (SpO2) of 95% in ambient air. Cardiovascular examination found her tachycardic with a pulse rate of 110 bpm and hypertensive at a blood pressure reading of 170/100 mmHg. Random fingerstick glucose was measured at the bedside revealing hyperglycemia at a level of around **14 mmol/L** **(252 mg/dL)**.
Urinalysis via dipstick test demonstrated glycosuria without accompanying ketonuria or proteinuria. Blood tests returned indicating mild leukocytosis (WBC count: **11 x10^9/L**) but without signs suggestive for infection such as fever or systemic evidence pointing towards sepsis.
Neurologically, no evidence pointed toward dominant hemispheric infarct—head CT scan did not depict acute intracranial bleeding nor significant signs associated with ischemia visually confirmed upon A 52-year-old Indian man, devoid of any significant personal medical or trauma history and not under any specific medical treatment, was referred to our hepatobiliary surgery department for the management of an unclear abdominal mass located in the right upper quadrant. His initial presentation dates back approximately two weeks prior with non-described fever and generalized malaise. As part of his febrile illness workup, a CT scan was performed which revealed the presence of an ill-defined mass.
Upon further discussion, the patient describes that his ailment began with mild fever accompanied by fatigue and body aches. Several days following these initial symptoms, he developed a progressive surge in discomfort localized to his right hypochondrium followed by intermittent episodes of more pronounced pain. Despite such symptoms, he did not report experiencing any gastrointestinal or respiratory symptoms nor could he recall any recent incidents involving trauma or physical exertion that might have explained such findings.
On clinical examination at our unit, this gentleman displayed a slightly elevated temperature (37.9°C), though remaining afebrile during most observed intervals. General examination highlighted pallor but no noticeable jaundice or cyanosis. Vital signs were otherwise unremarkable; blood pressure read within normal limits (128/80 mmHg), pulse regular at 82 beats per minute with good volume peripheral pulses.
His abdomen was soft overall but tender upon palpation predominantly around the right hypochondrium without rebound tenderness suggestive of peritoneal irritation; there appeared to be some guarding indicative of deeper discomfort rather than outright rigidity associated typically suggestive outright inflammation such as appendicitis etcetera — bowel sounds diffuse yet normoactive indicating well ongoing peristaltic activity throughout gut structures involved thus negating immediate ileus scenario depiction here essentially! An otherwise healthy 7-month-old male infant, the first child of two young parents without medical problems in their past medical history or a history of congenital malformations in their families, was admitted to our pediatric emergency department. His parents brought him in due to noticeable irritability and persistent crying over the last four hours. The father detailed that the infant had struggled with bowel movements for the past three days and noted that he had two episodes of vomiting earlier this morning. In addition, they mentioned that the child had sustained a head injury approximately five hours ago after rolling off a couch onto a hardwood floor.
Upon arrival at the hospital, the infant appeared agitated and inconsolable despite efforts by his parents. The physical examination revealed signs indicative of abdominal discomfort such as bloating and tenderness upon palpation. Further inquiries into his medical history indicated no previous significant illnesses or surgeries.
Vitals recorded on admission showed tachycardia with heart rate at 150 beats per minute, respiratory rate at 40 breaths per minute, a mild fever with body temperature measured at 37.8°C (100°F), and normal blood pressure for his age group.
The detailed neurological assessment didn't show any immediate abnormalities; however, considering the recent head trauma described by his parents, we recommended close observation along with scheduled imaging studies. Patient J.R.L. was a twelve-week-old male infant who presented with a history of a swelling in the right upper arm since birth and an 8-week history of intermittent fever. The swelling on the right upper arm was approximately the size of a pea at birth and progressively increased in size over time. At five weeks old, he was brought to a community clinic where healthcare providers suspected that the lesion was an abscess. It was incised, yielding thick yellow material presumed to be pus; however, despite this intervention, the swelling rapidly enlarged and exhibited delayed wound healing accompanied by erythema and tenderness.
Approximately two weeks after this initial incision, J.R.L.'s parents returned to the clinic concerned about his persistent fevers, skin discoloration around the swollen area, and frequent crying suggestive of pain or discomfort. Additional complications included poor appetite and mild weight loss over two weeks.
The infant had been born full-term following an uncomplicated pregnancy via spontaneous vaginal delivery attended by midwives at home under sterile conditions. There were no significant perinatal or neonatal complications reported. He had received all routine immunizations appropriate for his age but had not yet begun any elective vaccinations beyond that period due to logistical delays.
J.R.L.'s vaccination history excluded him from diseases such as Hepatitis B but noted vulnerability given incomplete later-stage inoculations pertinent at three months postpartum (like rotavirus). Family medical histories revealed no hereditary malignancies nor immunodeficiencies warranting particular scrutiny against genetic predisposition towards pathologies affecting integumentary growths manifestly typified herein case.
